\section{Mixed \texorpdfstring{$(E+O)$}{(E+O)} Branch: Exact Full Dirac Coefficients and the General Anisotropy Window}

\section{Short explanation}

We now release the pure-even restriction \(T_O=0\) and keep the full transverse vector
\[
T_*=(T_E,T_O).
\]
This gives the strongest exact reduced coefficient sheet currently available.

The key point is that the mixed branch changes the transverse orientation and transverse norm \(R_\perp\), but it does not remove the small-defect suppression factor
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\]
from the anisotropy ratio.

\section{1. Exact mixed-branch master symbol}

The submission-ready reduced symbol is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_1(p)
=
\frac12\,\alpha_X L_* I_{\theta'^3}\,p_\parallel\sigma_3
+
\beta_X I_\theta T_E\,(p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
+
\beta_X I_\theta T_O\,(-p_2\sigma_1+p_1\sigma_2),
}
\label{eq:mixed_A1_master}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*:=m_\parallel u_\parallel,
\qquad
T_*:=(T_E,T_O),
\qquad
R_\perp:=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2}.
}
\label{eq:mixed_LT}
\end{equation}

The explicit overlap factors are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_\theta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
I_{\theta'^3}
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3.
}
\label{eq:mixed_overlaps}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact coefficient sheet}

Therefore the exact reduced coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
\frac12\,\alpha_X L_* I_{\theta'^3}
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,\alpha_X L_*\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:mixed_lambda}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
\beta_X I_\theta T_E
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X T_E\,\theta_0\kappa,
}
\label{eq:mixed_alpha}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta
=
\beta_X I_\theta T_O
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X T_O\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:mixed_beta}
\end{equation}

Hence the exact nondegeneracy condition is simply
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*\neq 0,
\qquad
R_\perp>0.
}
\label{eq:mixed_nondeg}
\end{equation}
Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel\neq 0,
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)\neq(0,0).
}
\label{eq:mixed_nondeg_lab}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Polar form of the transverse sector}

Write the transverse vector in polar form:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
T_E=R_\perp\cos\varphi,
\qquad
T_O=R_\perp\sin\varphi.
}
\label{eq:mixed_polar}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
\beta_X I_\theta R_\perp\cos\varphi,
\qquad
\beta
=
\beta_X I_\theta R_\perp\sin\varphi.
}
\label{eq:mixed_alpha_beta_polar}
\end{equation}

So the transverse sector is completely controlled by two finite invariants:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_\perp
\quad\text{(magnitude)},
\qquad
\varphi
\quad\text{(orientation angle)}.
}
\label{eq:mixed_perp_data}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Full \(4\times4\) graded Dirac operator}

Introduce Pauli matrices \(\rho_a\) on the grading/valley factor and \(\sigma_a\) on the internal factor.
Then the full graded operator is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal D_1(p)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
0 & A_1(p)\\
A_1(p)^\dagger & 0
\end{pmatrix}
=
\rho_1\otimes A_1(p).
}
\label{eq:mixed_D1}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:mixed_A1_master}, one gets
\begin{align}
\boxed{
\mathcal D_1(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel\,\rho_1\otimes\sigma_3
+
(\alpha p_1-\beta p_2)\,\rho_1\otimes\sigma_1
+
(\alpha p_2+\beta p_1)\,\rho_1\otimes\sigma_2.
}
\label{eq:mixed_D1_explicit}
\end{align}

\section{5. Exact dispersion relation}

Because the three Pauli directions anticommute, the dispersion is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
E(p)^2
=
\lambda_\parallel^2 p_\parallel^2
+
(\alpha^2+\beta^2)(p_1^2+p_2^2).
}
\label{eq:mixed_dispersion}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:mixed_alpha}--\eqref{eq:mixed_beta},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha^2+\beta^2
=
\left(\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\right)^2
\beta_X^2
R_\perp^2
\theta_0^2\kappa^2.
}
\label{eq:mixed_alpha_beta_norm}
\end{equation}

Thus the infrared transverse speed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\sqrt{\alpha^2+\beta^2}
=
|\beta_X I_\theta|\,R_\perp.
}
\label{eq:mixed_vperp}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Exact anisotropy ratio}

The infrared longitudinal speed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\lambda_\parallel
=
\frac12\,\alpha_X L_* I_{\theta'^3},
}
\label{eq:mixed_vpar}
\end{equation}
so the exact anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho
=
\frac{v_\parallel^{\mathrm{IR}}}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}}
=
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\frac{L_*}{2R_\perp}
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta},
}
\label{eq:mixed_rho_exact}
\end{equation}
up to the overall sign convention.

Using
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}
=
\frac{3}{16}\,\theta_0^2\kappa^2,
}
\label{eq:mixed_overlap_ratio}
\end{equation}
we obtain the explicit leading law
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
\frac{L_*}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixed_rho_LO}
\end{equation}

This is the exact mixed-branch upgrade of the pure-even anisotropy law.

\section{7. Isotropy window in the mixed branch}

Introduce the dimensionless mixed-branch control parameter
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal C_{\mathrm{mix}}
:=
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\frac{|L_*|}{R_\perp}.
}
\label{eq:mixed_Cmix}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\mathcal C_{\mathrm{mix}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixed_rho_abs}
\end{equation}

Therefore the exact isotropy condition \( |\rho|\sim1 \) becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\sim
\frac{32}{3}\,
\frac{1}{\mathcal C_{\mathrm{mix}}}
=
\frac{32}{3}\,
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{R_\perp}{|L_*|}.
}
\label{eq:mixed_iso_condition}
\end{equation}

More generally, for a \(K=O(1)\) isotropy band
\[
\frac1K\le |\rho|\le K,
\]
the necessary and sufficient condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{32}{3K}
\le
\mathcal C_{\mathrm{mix}}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\le
\frac{32K}{3}.
}
\label{eq:mixed_iso_band}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Main structural conclusion}

The mixed branch changes the transverse orientation angle \(\varphi\) and enlarges the transverse magnitude from the pure-even scalar \(T\) to the full norm \(R_\perp\).
However, it does not remove the small-defect suppression factor:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho|\propto (\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixed_core_conclusion}
\end{equation}

So the mixed branch can broaden the isotropy window by increasing \(R_\perp\) or by rotating the transverse sector, but isotropy is still not automatic in the natural small-defect regime.

\section{9. Honest endpoint}

Therefore the strongest honest current mixed-branch frontier is reduced to the finite data
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*,
\qquad
R_\perp,
\qquad
\varphi,
\qquad
\alpha_X/\beta_X.
}
\label{eq:mixed_leftover}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(L_*,T_E,T_O,\alpha_X,\beta_X)
\longmapsto
(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta,\rho).
}
\label{eq:mixed_final_map}
\end{equation}

No new structural theorem is missing at this stage.

\section{Final Summary}

In the mixed \((E+O)\) branch, the exact reduced symbol is
\[
\boxed{
A_1(p)
=
\frac12\,\alpha_X L_* I_{\theta'^3}\,p_\parallel\sigma_3
+
\beta_X I_\theta T_E\,(p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
+
\beta_X I_\theta T_O\,(-p_2\sigma_1+p_1\sigma_2).
}
\]

The exact coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,\alpha_X L_*\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X T_E\,\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X T_O\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\]

Defining
\[
\boxed{
R_\perp=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2},
\qquad
T_E=R_\perp\cos\varphi,
\qquad
T_O=R_\perp\sin\varphi,
}
\]
the infrared transverse speed is
\[
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}=|\beta_X I_\theta|\,R_\perp.
}
\]

Hence the exact anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
\rho
=
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\frac{L_*}{2R_\perp}
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta},
}
\]
and using
\[
\boxed{
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}=\frac{3}{16}\theta_0^2\kappa^2,
}
\]
the leading law becomes
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
\frac{L_*}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Therefore the exact isotropy condition in the mixed branch is
\[
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\sim
\frac{32}{3}\,
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{R_\perp}{|L_*|}.
}
\]

So the mixed branch can enlarge the isotropy window through the full transverse norm \(R_\perp\), but it does not remove the fundamental small-defect suppression factor \((\theta_0\kappa)^2\).

Hence the strongest honest remaining frontier is the finite extraction of
\[
\boxed{
L_*,\qquad T_E,\qquad T_O,\qquad \alpha_X,\qquad \beta_X.
}
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\boxed{
(L_*,T_E,T_O,\alpha_X,\beta_X)
\longmapsto
(\lambda_\parallel,\alpha,\beta,\rho).
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Canonical Mixed-Branch Sample and the Explicit Size of \texorpdfstring{$R_\perp/|L_*|$}{Rperp/Lstar}}

\section{Short explanation}

We now evaluate the smallest nontrivial mixed \((E+O)\) sample:
both transverse channels are turned on with equal order-one size.

This gives the cleanest explicit answer to the question:
how much can the mixed branch enlarge the isotropy window relative to the pure-even branch?

\section{1. Canonical mixed sample}

Take the minimal real order-one sample
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta=c=u_3=m_\parallel=1,
\qquad
T_E=1,
\qquad
T_O=1.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_input}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*=m_\parallel u_3=1,
}
\label{eq:mixsample_L}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_\perp=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2}=\sqrt2.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_R}
\end{equation}

Therefore the mixed-branch gain factor is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{R_\perp}{|L_*|}=\sqrt2,
\qquad
\frac{|L_*|}{R_\perp}=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_ratio}
\end{equation}

So, compared with the pure-even choice \(T_O=0,\ T_E=1\), the mixed branch enlarges the transverse norm by a factor \(\sqrt2\), but no more in this canonical equal-amplitude sample.

\section{2. Bloch-overlap scalar}

For the minimal real ansatz
\[
E_\pm(0)=\varepsilon_\pm\mathbf 1_2+\delta\Sigma_3+c\Sigma_1,
\]
the overlap scalar is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{00}=\frac{\delta}{\sqrt{\delta^2+c^2}}.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_Delta}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:mixsample_input},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_{00}=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_Delta_num}
\end{equation}

Thus the longitudinal overlap remains order one.

\section{3. Exact mixed-branch coefficients}

Using the exact overlap values
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_\theta=-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
I_{\theta'^3}=-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:mixsample_overlaps}
\end{equation}
the coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,\alpha_X L_*\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:mixsample_lambda}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X T_E\,\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X T_O\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_ab}
\end{equation}

With \eqref{eq:mixsample_input}, this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,\alpha_X\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:mixsample_lambda_num}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X\,\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_ab_num}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Transverse speed}

The transverse speed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\sqrt{\alpha^2+\beta^2}
=
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,|\beta_X|\,R_\perp\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_vperp}
\end{equation}

Hence, using \(R_\perp=\sqrt2\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\frac{\sqrt6\,\pi}{16}\,|\beta_X|\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_vperp_num}
\end{equation}

Numerically,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\approx 0.0319,
\qquad
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\approx 0.340,
\qquad
\frac{\sqrt6\pi}{16}\approx 0.481.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_nums}
\end{equation}

So the canonical mixed sample gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel \approx -0.0319\,\alpha_X\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:mixsample_lambda_num2}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha\approx -0.340\,\beta_X\,\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta\approx -0.340\,\beta_X\,\theta_0\kappa,
}
\label{eq:mixsample_ab_num2}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}\approx 0.481\,|\beta_X|\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_vperp_num2}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Anisotropy ratio}

The exact mixed-branch ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho
=
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
\frac{L_*}{2R_\perp}\,
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_rho_exact}
\end{equation}

Using
\[
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}=\frac{3}{16}\theta_0^2\kappa^2,
\]
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
\frac{L_*}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_rho_LO}
\end{equation}

With the canonical mixed sample \eqref{eq:mixsample_ratio},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt2}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_rho_final}
\end{equation}

Numerically,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{3}{32\sqrt2}\approx 0.0663.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_rho_coeff}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
\approx
0.0663\,
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_rho_num}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Comparison with the pure-even sample}

For the pure-even canonical sample \(T_E=1,\ T_O=0\), one had
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{even}}
=
\frac{3}{32}\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.09375
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_rho_even}
\end{equation}

So the equal-amplitude mixed branch yields
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{mixed}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{even}}.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_compare}
\end{equation}

Therefore the mixed branch does not remove the small-defect suppression.
In this canonical sample, it actually makes the cone more transverse-dominant by increasing \(R_\perp\).

\section{7. Isotropy window}

The exact isotropy condition \(|\rho|\sim1\) becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\sim
\frac{32\sqrt2}{3}
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
}
\label{eq:mixsample_iso}
\end{equation}
for the canonical equal-amplitude mixed sample.

Since
\[
\frac{32\sqrt2}{3}\approx 15.08,
\]
one obtains
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\sim
15.1\,
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|.
}
\label{eq:mixsample_iso_num}
\end{equation}

So if \(\alpha_X\) and \(\beta_X\) are of the same microscopic order, isotropy requires
\[
\theta_0\kappa=O(1),
\]
not the natural small-defect regime.

\section{8. Final conclusion of the canonical mixed sample}

The canonical mixed branch with
\[
T_E=T_O=1
\]
does not open a generic isotropic window.

It preserves the exact full Dirac structure, but the anisotropy ratio remains
\[
|\rho|\propto (\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\]
with a smaller prefactor than in the pure-even branch.

Therefore isotropy still requires a tuned longitudinal enhancement relative to the transverse norm.

\section{Final Summary}

For the canonical equal-amplitude mixed sample
\[
\delta=c=u_3=m_\parallel=1,
\qquad
T_E=T_O=1,
\]
one obtains
\[
\boxed{
L_*=1,
\qquad
R_\perp=\sqrt2,
\qquad
\Delta_{00}=\frac1{\sqrt2}.
}
\]

The exact reduced coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,\alpha_X\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3
\approx
-0.0319\,\alpha_X\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\alpha=\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X\,\theta_0\kappa
\approx
-0.340\,\beta_X\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\]

The transverse speed is
\[
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\frac{\sqrt6\pi}{16}|\beta_X|\,\theta_0\kappa
\approx
0.481\,|\beta_X|\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\]

Hence the anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt2}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.0663\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Therefore the equal-amplitude mixed branch satisfies
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{mixed}}
=
\frac{1}{\sqrt2}
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|_{\mathrm{even}}.
}
\]

So the mixed branch does not generically restore isotropy.
In the canonical sample it increases the transverse norm and makes the cone more anisotropic in the longitudinal/transverse sense.

The exact isotropy condition remains
\[
\boxed{
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\sim
\frac{32}{3}
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{R_\perp}{|L_*|},
}
\]
which is still a tuned balance rather than a generic consequence of the mixed branch.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Exact Reduction of the Mixed-Branch Isotropy Problem to a Bias-Fraction Inequality}

\section{Short explanation}

The remaining issue is not the Dirac architecture itself.
It is the size of the ratio
\[
\frac{R_\perp}{|L_*|}
=
\frac{\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2}}{|m_\parallel u_\parallel|}.
\]

To make this executable, we separate the shell-normal intervalley block into its total internal size and its longitudinal bias fraction.

\section{1. Pauli decomposition of the shell-normal block}

Write the shell-normal intervalley block as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_\parallel
=
u_0\mathbf 1_2
+
u_1\Sigma_1
+
u_2\Sigma_2
+
u_3\Sigma_3,
\qquad
u_0,u_1,u_2,u_3\in\mathbb R.
}
\label{eq:bias_Upar}
\end{equation}

Define the traceless internal size
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_U:=\sqrt{u_1^2+u_2^2+u_3^2}.
}
\label{eq:bias_QU}
\end{equation}

The longitudinal shell-normal scalar is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
u_\parallel
=
\frac12\Tr(\Sigma_3U_\parallel)
=
u_3.
}
\label{eq:bias_upar}
\end{equation}

So the longitudinal invariant is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*
=
m_\parallel u_3.
}
\label{eq:bias_Lstar}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Bias fraction}

Introduce the dimensionless longitudinal bias fraction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi_U
:=
\frac{|u_3|}{Q_U},
\qquad
0\le \chi_U\le 1.
}
\label{eq:bias_chiU}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|u_3|=\chi_U Q_U,
\qquad
|L_*|=|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,Q_U.
}
\label{eq:bias_Lstar_factor}
\end{equation}

This is exact.
So the mixed-branch longitudinal problem is reduced to two pieces:
\[
Q_U
\quad\text{(total shell-normal internal size)},
\qquad
\chi_U
\quad\text{(fraction of that size aligned with }\Sigma_3\text{)}.
\]

\section{3. Transverse norm}

Define the exact transverse norm
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_\perp:=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2}.
}
\label{eq:bias_Rperp}
\end{equation}

Thus the key ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{R_\perp}{|L_*|}
=
\frac{R_\perp}{|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,Q_U}.
}
\label{eq:bias_ratio_exact}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|L_*|}{R_\perp}
=
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}.
}
\label{eq:bias_ratio_inverse}
\end{equation}

So isotropy depends on three independent ingredients:
\[
|m_\parallel|,
\qquad
\chi_U,
\qquad
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}.
\]

\section{4. Exact anisotropy law in bias-fraction form}

From the exact mixed-branch formula,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho
=
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\frac{L_*}{2R_\perp}
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta},
}
\label{eq:bias_rho_exact}
\end{equation}
and using
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}
=
\frac{3}{16}\theta_0^2\kappa^2,
}
\label{eq:bias_overlap_ratio}
\end{equation}
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32}
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\frac{L_*}{R_\perp}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:bias_rho_LO}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:bias_ratio_inverse},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32}
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\cdot \operatorname{sgn}(m_\parallel u_3).
}
\label{eq:bias_rho_factorized}
\end{equation}

Hence, in magnitude,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:bias_rho_abs}
\end{equation}

This is the exact reduction of the isotropy problem.

\section{5. Necessary condition for isotropy}

Suppose one asks for an \(O(1)\) isotropy window,
\[
|\rho|\sim 1.
\]
Then a necessary condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\gtrsim
\frac{32}{3}
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{1}{|m_\parallel|\chi_U}
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:bias_iso_necessary}
\end{equation}

This shows explicitly:
if \(\theta_0\kappa\ll1\), isotropy requires a large enhancement of
\[
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\quad\text{or}\quad
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\quad\text{or}\quad
|m_\parallel|\chi_U.
\]

\section{6. Sufficient condition for strong anisotropy}

Conversely, if there exists a finite constant \(C_{\mathrm{mix}}\) such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\le C_{\mathrm{mix}},
}
\label{eq:bias_Cmix}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
\le
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
C_{\mathrm{mix}}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:bias_aniso_bound}
\end{equation}

Therefore, under any non-hierarchical mixed truncation with
\[
C_{\mathrm{mix}}=O(1),
\]
one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll1
\qquad\text{for}\qquad
|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1.
}
\label{eq:bias_small_defect_aniso}
\end{equation}

So strong anisotropy remains generic in the small-defect regime unless a parametric enhancement is present.

\section{7. Exact band form for the isotropy window}

For an \(O(1)\) isotropy band
\[
\frac1K\le |\rho|\le K,
\qquad
K=O(1),
\]
the exact necessary and sufficient condition becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{32}{3K}
\le
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\le
\frac{32K}{3}.
}
\label{eq:bias_isoband}
\end{equation}

This is the cleanest executable form of the mixed-branch isotropy criterion.

\section{8. Structural verdict}

The mixed branch does not remove the fundamental defect suppression factor
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
\]
What it does is to replace the pure-even scalar \(T\) by the full transverse norm \(R_\perp\), and to expose the longitudinal side as the product
\[
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,Q_U.
\]

Therefore the real question is not whether \(T_O\neq0\) exists.
It is whether the refined locked truncation can generate a parametrically large ratio
\[
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\]
or a parametrically large bias fraction \(\chi_U\).

Without such enhancement, the mixed branch remains naturally anisotropic.

\section{Final Summary}

Write
\[
U_\parallel
=
u_0\mathbf 1_2+u_1\Sigma_1+u_2\Sigma_2+u_3\Sigma_3,
\qquad
Q_U:=\sqrt{u_1^2+u_2^2+u_3^2},
\qquad
\chi_U:=\frac{|u_3|}{Q_U}.
\]

Then
\[
\boxed{
|L_*|=|m_\parallel|\,|u_3|
=
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,Q_U.
}
\]

With
\[
R_\perp=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2},
\]
the exact mixed-branch anisotropy law becomes
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Therefore isotropy requires the necessary condition
\[
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\gtrsim
\frac{32}{3}
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{1}{|m_\parallel|\chi_U}
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\]

Conversely, if there exists \(C_{\mathrm{mix}}=O(1)\) such that
\[
\boxed{
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\le C_{\mathrm{mix}},
}
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
\le
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
C_{\mathrm{mix}}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Hence, without a genuine enhancement in \(Q_U/R_\perp\) or in \(|m_\parallel|\chi_U\), the mixed branch remains naturally anisotropic in the small-defect regime.

The exact isotropy band condition is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{32}{3K}
\le
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\le
\frac{32K}{3}.
}
\]

Therefore the remaining microscopic frontier is fully reduced to the finite question of whether the refined locked truncation can generate a parametrically large ratio
\[
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}.
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Shell-Order Comparison of \(Q_U\) and \(R_\perp\): Why Isotropy is Not Generic in the Mixed Branch}

\section{Short explanation}

The remaining question is whether the mixed branch can produce a parametrically large ratio
\[
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp},
\qquad
Q_U:=\sqrt{u_1^2+u_2^2+u_3^2},
\qquad
R_\perp:=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2}.
\]

At the level of the current refined locked first-shell truncation, both \(u=(u_1,u_2,u_3)\) and \(T=(T_E,T_O)\) are finite linear functions of the same underlying shell-amplitude data.
Therefore the problem reduces to a finite-dimensional ratio of two linear maps.

This gives an honest structural verdict:
\(Q_U/R_\perp\) is generically \(O(1)\) away from transverse-cancellation loci, and can become parametrically large only by tuning toward \(R_\perp=0\).

\section{1. Finite shell-amplitude vector}

Let \(X\in \mathbb R^N\) denote the real finite vector of first-shell refined-locked amplitude data entering the projected intervalley and transverse blocks.
Concretely, \(X\) collects the independent real and imaginary parts of the relevant shell Fourier coefficients surviving the chosen truncation.

Then there exist fixed real matrices
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_U\in \mathrm{Mat}_{3\times N}(\mathbb R),
\qquad
A_T\in \mathrm{Mat}_{2\times N}(\mathbb R),
}
\label{eq:QT_matrices}
\end{equation}
such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
u=(u_1,u_2,u_3)=A_U X,
\qquad
T=(T_E,T_O)=A_T X.
}
\label{eq:QT_linear_maps}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q_U=\|A_U X\|_2,
\qquad
R_\perp=\|A_T X\|_2.
}
\label{eq:QT_norms}
\end{equation}

This is exact at the fixed truncation level.

\section{2. Ratio as a finite-dimensional norm quotient}

Restrict to the unit sphere
\[
S^{N-1}:=\{X\in \mathbb R^N:\|X\|_2=1\}.
\]
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
=
\frac{\|A_U X\|_2}{\|A_T X\|_2},
\qquad
X\in S^{N-1},\quad A_T X\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:QT_ratio}
\end{equation}

Since \(A_U\) and \(A_T\) are fixed matrices, this is a continuous function on
\[
S^{N-1}\setminus \ker(A_T).
\]

Therefore, on any compact subset away from the transverse-cancellation locus \(\ker(A_T)\), the ratio is automatically bounded above and below by finite constants.

\section{3. Generic \(O(1)\) region}

Fix positive constants \(\varepsilon_T,\varepsilon_U>0\), and define the shell-generic region
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Omega_{\varepsilon_T,\varepsilon_U}
:=
\left\{
X\in S^{N-1}:
\|A_T X\|_2\ge \varepsilon_T,
\;
\|A_U X\|_2\ge \varepsilon_U
\right\}.
}
\label{eq:QT_generic_region}
\end{equation}

On \(\Omega_{\varepsilon_T,\varepsilon_U}\), one has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\varepsilon_U}{\|A_T\|_{\mathrm{op}}}
\le
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\le
\frac{\|A_U\|_{\mathrm{op}}}{\varepsilon_T}.
}
\label{eq:QT_bounds_generic}
\end{equation}

Hence,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}=O(1)
\qquad
\text{throughout the shell-generic region.}
}
\label{eq:QT_O1_generic}
\end{equation}

This is the strongest honest generic statement available without additional microscopic input.

\section{4. Where can parametrically large \texorpdfstring{$Q_U/R_\perp$}{QU/Rperp} arise?}

From \eqref{eq:QT_ratio}, a parametrically large value of \(Q_U/R_\perp\) can occur only if
\[
\|A_T X\|_2=R_\perp
\]
becomes parametrically small while \(\|A_U X\|_2\) stays nonzero.

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\to\infty
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
X\to \ker(A_T)
\;\text{ with }\;
A_U X\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:QT_large_ratio}
\end{equation}

Thus the only structural route to a large enhancement is approach to the transverse-cancellation locus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ker(A_T)=\{X:T_E(X)=0,\ T_O(X)=0\}.
}
\label{eq:QT_kernelT}
\end{equation}

This is a tuning locus, not a generic region.

\section{5. Where can parametrically small \texorpdfstring{$Q_U/R_\perp$}{QU/Rperp} arise?}

Similarly,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\to 0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
X\to \ker(A_U)
\;\text{ with }\;
A_T X\neq 0.
}
\label{eq:QT_small_ratio}
\end{equation}

So strong transverse dominance arises near the longitudinal-cancellation locus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ker(A_U)=\{X:u_1(X)=u_2(X)=u_3(X)=0\}.
}
\label{eq:QT_kernelU}
\end{equation}

Again, this is a tuning locus.

\section{6. Bias-fraction form of the isotropy law}

Recall the exact factorization
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|L_*|
=
|m_\parallel|\,|u_3|
=
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,Q_U,
\qquad
\chi_U:=\frac{|u_3|}{Q_U}\in[0,1].
}
\label{eq:QT_Lstar_bias}
\end{equation}

Then the mixed-branch anisotropy law becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:QT_rho_bias}
\end{equation}

Therefore, on the shell-generic region \(\Omega_{\varepsilon_T,\varepsilon_U}\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
O\!\left(
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
|m_\parallel|\chi_U
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\right).
}
\label{eq:QT_rho_generic}
\end{equation}

In particular, if
\[
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|=O(1),
\qquad
|m_\parallel|=O(1),
\qquad
\chi_U=O(1),
\qquad
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}=O(1),
\]
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
O\!\bigl((\theta_0\kappa)^2\bigr).
}
\label{eq:QT_rho_smalldef}
\end{equation}

So the mixed branch remains naturally anisotropic in the small-defect regime.

\section{7. Necessary condition for isotropy}

Demanding \(|\rho|\sim 1\) gives the exact necessary condition
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\gtrsim
\frac{32}{3}
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{1}{|m_\parallel|\chi_U}
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:QT_iso_necessary}
\end{equation}

Thus, if \(|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1\), isotropy requires at least one of the following:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(i) } \frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\gg 1,
\qquad
\text{or (ii) } \left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|\gg 1,
\qquad
\text{or (iii) } |m_\parallel|\chi_U\gg 1.
}
\label{eq:QT_iso_routes}
\end{equation}

Without such enhancement, isotropy is impossible in the small-defect regime.

\section{8. Sufficient condition for generic anisotropy}

Conversely, if there exists a finite shell-order constant \(C_{\mathrm{shell}}\) such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\le C_{\mathrm{shell}},
}
\label{eq:QT_Cshell}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
\le
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
C_{\mathrm{shell}}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:QT_rho_upper}
\end{equation}

Therefore, if
\[
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|=O(1),
\qquad
C_{\mathrm{shell}}=O(1),
\]
one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll1
\qquad
\text{for}\qquad
|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1.
}
\label{eq:QT_generic_aniso}
\end{equation}

This is the strongest honest generic verdict currently available.

\section{9. Structural verdict}

The mixed branch does not automatically restore isotropy.

Its only real advantage over the pure-even branch is that it exposes the full transverse norm \(R_\perp\) and the full shell-normal internal size \(Q_U\).
But unless the refined locked truncation generates a genuine enhancement of
\[
Q_U/R_\perp,
\]
or of \(|\alpha_X/\beta_X|\), or of \(|m_\parallel|\chi_U\), the factor
\[
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\]
still forces the branch into the anisotropic regime for small defects.

So the remaining microscopic question is now fully finite:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Does the actual refined locked truncation place us near }\ker(A_T)\text{ or not?}
}
\label{eq:QT_last_question}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

Let
\[
u=(u_1,u_2,u_3)=A_U X,
\qquad
T=(T_E,T_O)=A_T X,
\]
for the finite first-shell refined-locked amplitude vector \(X\in\mathbb R^N\).
Then
\[
\boxed{
Q_U=\|A_U X\|_2,
\qquad
R_\perp=\|A_T X\|_2.
}
\]

Introduce
\[
\boxed{
\chi_U=\frac{|u_3|}{Q_U},
\qquad
|L_*|=|m_\parallel|\chi_U Q_U.
}
\]

Then the mixed-branch anisotropy law becomes
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
\,|m_\parallel|\,\chi_U\,
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

On any shell-generic region away from the transverse-cancellation locus \(\ker(A_T)\), the ratio \(Q_U/R_\perp\) is bounded:
\[
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}=O(1).
}
\]

Therefore, if
\[
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|=O(1),
\qquad
|m_\parallel|=O(1),
\qquad
\chi_U=O(1),
\qquad
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}=O(1),
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=O\bigl((\theta_0\kappa)^2\bigr),
}
\]
so the mixed branch remains naturally anisotropic in the small-defect regime.

The exact necessary condition for isotropy is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
\gtrsim
\frac{32}{3}
\left|\frac{\beta_X}{\alpha_X}\right|
\frac{1}{|m_\parallel|\chi_U}
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\]

Hence isotropy requires a genuine enhancement of at least one among
\[
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp},
\qquad
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|,
\qquad
|m_\parallel|\chi_U.
}
\]

Without such enhancement, the mixed branch does not generically restore isotropy.

\section{Rank of the Transverse Map and the Structure of the Cancellation Locus}

\section{Short explanation}

We now determine whether the transverse cancellation condition
\[
A_T X = 0
\]
can be satisfied generically within the first-shell refined locked truncation.

This reduces to computing the rank of the linear map
\[
A_T:\mathbb{R}^N \to \mathbb{R}^2,
\qquad
X \mapsto (T_E(X),T_O(X)).
\]

\section{1. Structure of the transverse map}

The transverse coefficients are linear in the shell amplitudes:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
T_E = \sum_{i=1}^N a_i X_i,
\qquad
T_O = \sum_{i=1}^N b_i X_i.
}
\label{eq:AT_components}
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_T =
\begin{pmatrix}
a_1 & a_2 & \cdots & a_N \\
b_1 & b_2 & \cdots & b_N
\end{pmatrix}.
}
\label{eq:AT_matrix}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Linear independence of \(T_E\) and \(T_O\)}

The even and odd transverse structures originate from different symmetry channels:
\[
(p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
\quad \text{vs} \quad
(-p_2\sigma_1+p_1\sigma_2).
\]

These correspond to orthogonal SO(2) representations in the transverse plane.

Therefore, unless symmetry forces a degeneracy,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
T_E \not\propto T_O.
}
\label{eq:TE_TO_independent}
\end{equation}

Hence the rows of \(A_T\) are linearly independent, and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathrm{rank}(A_T)=2.
}
\label{eq:AT_rank2}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Kernel dimension}

By rank–nullity,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\dim \ker(A_T)
=
N - 2.
}
\label{eq:AT_kernel_dim}
\end{equation}

Thus the transverse cancellation locus is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ker(A_T)
=
\{X \in \mathbb{R}^N : T_E(X)=0,\ T_O(X)=0\}.
}
\label{eq:AT_kernel}
\end{equation}

This is a codimension-2 subspace.

\section{4. Measure and genericity}

Inside the unit sphere \(S^{N-1}\), the kernel has dimension
\[
(N-2)-1 = N-3,
\]
while the full sphere has dimension \(N-1\).

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{codimension of } \ker(A_T)\cap S^{N-1} = 2.
}
\label{eq:AT_codim2}
\end{equation}

Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ker(A_T) \text{ is a measure-zero subset.}
}
\label{eq:AT_measure_zero}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Consequence for } \texorpdfstring{$Q_U/R_\perp$}{QU/Rperp}

From
\[
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp} = \frac{\|A_U X\|}{\|A_T X\|},
\]
we see that divergence requires \(A_T X \to 0\).

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp} \to \infty
\quad \Longleftrightarrow \quad
X \to \ker(A_T).
}
\label{eq:ratio_divergence}
\end{equation}

Thus large enhancement is only possible near a codimension-2 tuning locus.

\section{6. Final implication for isotropy}

Recall
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\left|\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\right|
|m_\parallel|\chi_U
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\end{equation}

Since
\[
\frac{Q_U}{R_\perp}=O(1)
\]
generically, and diverges only on a codimension-2 locus, we obtain:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Isotropy requires tuning toward } \ker(A_T).
}
\end{equation}

Thus:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The mixed branch does not generically produce an isotropic Dirac cone.}
}
\end{equation}

Instead, it produces an anisotropic cone in the generic small-defect regime.

\section{7. Structural verdict}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Isotropic emergent Dirac is a measure-zero phenomenon in the mixed branch.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Naturalness of the Coupling Hierarchy in the One-Mediator UV Completion}

\section{Short explanation}

The physically relevant question is not whether the reduced Dirac operator exists.
That part is already closed.

The remaining issue is whether the longitudinal coupling can be naturally enhanced enough to compete with the transverse sector, i.e. whether
\[
|\rho|\sim 1
\]
can arise generically from the microscopic UV couplings.

For the explicit one-mediator truncation, this becomes a question about the relative size of
\[
\alpha_X,\qquad \gamma_E,\qquad \gamma_O,
\]
because the common factor \(\beta_X/M_X^2\) cancels from the anisotropy ratio.

\section{1. One-mediator truncation and relevant couplings}

The explicit one-mediator truncation is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{trunc}}
=
\mathcal L_{\Psi}^{\mathrm{core}}
+
\mathcal L_{P}^{\mathrm{geom}}
+
\mathcal L_{\sigma}^{\mathrm{vol}}
+
\frac12 M_X^2 X_i^aX_i^a
+
\alpha_X X_i^a J_i^a
+
\beta_X X_i^a K_i^a
+
\gamma_E X_i^a \mathcal E_i^a
+
\gamma_O X_i^a \mathcal O_i^a.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_trunc}
\end{equation}

Integrating out \(X_i^a\) gives the relevant mixed terms
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
\supset
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}J_i^aK_i^a
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}K_i^a\mathcal E_i^a
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}K_i^a\mathcal O_i^a.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_cross}
\end{equation}

Thus the longitudinal branch is controlled by \(\alpha_X\beta_X\), while the transverse branch is controlled by \(\beta_X\gamma_E\) and \(\beta_X\gamma_O\). 

\section{2. Exact leading anisotropy ratio}

The explicit leading anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_rho}
\end{equation}

This is exact at the level of the one-mediator truncation and shows immediately that the common factor \(\beta_X/M_X^2\) cancels out of \(\rho\). Therefore the size of \(\rho\) is controlled only by

\[
\alpha_X : \gamma_E : \gamma_O
\]
and by the overlap-calibration constants
\[
C_\parallel,\qquad C_E,\qquad C_O.
\]

In canonical explicit normalization,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\parallel=C_E=C_O=1,
}
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_rho_canonical}
\end{equation}
This formula is already explicitly stated in the uploaded notes. 

\section{3. Unified-current hypothesis}

Assume the mediator couples to a unified shell-current multiplet
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathbb J_i^a
=
c_\parallel J_i^a
+
c_E\mathcal E_i^a
+
c_O\mathcal O_i^a,
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_unified_current}
\end{equation}
through a single microscopic coupling \(g_X\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}
=
g_X X_i^a\mathbb J_i^a.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_unified_int}
\end{equation}

Then the three effective couplings are not independent:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_projected}
\end{equation}

Substituting into \eqref{eq:UV_nat_rho}, we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{c_\parallel C_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2 C_E^2+c_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_rho_projected}
\end{equation}

In canonical normalization,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_rho_projected_canonical}
\end{equation}

Therefore the overall microscopic strength \(g_X\) cancels exactly.

\section{4. O(1) projection hypothesis and naturalness}

Assume the projection coefficients satisfy the natural \(O(1)\) hypothesis:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_\parallel=O(1),
\qquad
c_E=O(1),
\qquad
c_O=O(1),
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_O1}
\end{equation}
with no special microscopic symmetry that singles out the longitudinal direction.

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}=O(1).
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_ratio_O1}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
O\!\bigl((\theta_0\kappa)^2\bigr).
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_small_defect}
\end{equation}

Therefore, in the natural small-defect regime \(|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll1.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_aniso}
\end{equation}

So the one-mediator branch is naturally anisotropic unless the longitudinal channel is specially enhanced.

\section{5. Exact condition for isotropy from the UV couplings}

Demanding \(|\rho|\sim1\) in canonical normalization gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
\sim
\frac{32}{3}\,
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_iso_condition}
\end{equation}

Equivalently, before imposing the unified-current identification,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{|\alpha_X|}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}
\sim
\frac{32}{3}\,
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_iso_condition_raw}
\end{equation}

Thus isotropy requires a hierarchy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\alpha_X|
\gg
\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_hierarchy}
\end{equation}
if \(|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1\).

This is not generated by the unified-current hypothesis with \(O(1)\) projection coefficients.

\section{6. Honest verdict}

Therefore the strongest honest conclusion is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Within the one-mediator UV completion, isotropy is not natural under unified-current }O(1)\text{ projections.}
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_verdict1}
\end{equation}

Instead,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{generic small-defect configurations produce }|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll1.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_verdict2}
\end{equation}

To obtain \(|\rho|\sim1\), one must invoke at least one of the following:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(i) a genuine hierarchy } |\alpha_X|\gg\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2},
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_route1}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(ii) a non-small defect } |\theta_0\kappa|=O(1),
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_route2}
\end{equation}
or
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(iii) a separate microscopic mechanism that suppresses }c_E,c_O\text{ relative to }c_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:UV_nat_route3}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Consequence for the current TECT branch}

Hence the present strongest explicit working branch predicts:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{anisotropic Dirac is generic;}
}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{near-isotropic Dirac requires additional microscopic structure beyond the minimal natural one-mediator branch.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

In the explicit one-mediator truncation, the relevant mixed effective operators are
\[
-\frac{\alpha_X\beta_X}{M_X^2}J\cdot K,
\qquad
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_E}{M_X^2}K\cdot\mathcal E,
\qquad
-\frac{\beta_X\gamma_O}{M_X^2}K\cdot\mathcal O.
\]

Therefore the leading anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

In canonical normalization,
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Under the unified-current hypothesis
\[
\mathbb J_i^a=c_\parallel J_i^a+c_E\mathcal E_i^a+c_O\mathcal O_i^a,
\qquad
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}=g_X X_i^a\mathbb J_i^a,
\]
the couplings satisfy
\[
\boxed{
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O.
}
\]

Hence
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{c_\parallel C_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2 C_E^2+c_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\]
and in canonical normalization,
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Therefore, if the projection coefficients are natural \(O(1)\) numbers,
\[
\boxed{
c_\parallel,\ c_E,\ c_O = O(1),
}
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=O\bigl((\theta_0\kappa)^2\bigr)\ll1
\quad\text{for}\quad |\theta_0\kappa|\ll1.
}
\]

So isotropy is not natural in the minimal one-mediator branch.

To obtain \(|\rho|\sim1\), one needs at least one of:
\[
\boxed{
|\alpha_X|\gg\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2},
\qquad
|\theta_0\kappa|=O(1),
\qquad
\text{or an additional microscopic selection suppressing }c_E,c_O.
}
\]

Hence the strongest honest current verdict is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{anisotropic Dirac is generic in the minimal explicit TECT branch,}
}
\]
while
\[
\boxed{
\text{near-isotropic Dirac requires extra microscopic structure or tuning.}
}
\]

\section{Microscopic Realization of a Longitudinal-Only Selection Rule}

\section{Short explanation}

We investigate whether it is possible to naturally realize
\[
c_E,c_O \ll c_\parallel,
\]
i.e. a mediator that couples dominantly to the longitudinal current \(J_i^a\)
while suppressing the transverse structures \(\mathcal E_i^a,\mathcal O_i^a\).

The key point is that, in the minimal setup, all three objects belong to the same tensor class, so symmetry alone does not separate them.

\section{1. Tensor classification of the currents}

All currents have the same index structure:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_i^a,\quad \mathcal E_i^a,\quad \mathcal O_i^a
\in (\mathbf{3}_{\text{space}})\otimes (\mathbf{adj}_{\text{internal}}).
}
\label{eq:sel_tensor_class}
\end{equation}

Thus any rotationally invariant coupling of the form
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X_i^a \mathbb J_i^a
}
\end{equation}
cannot distinguish between these channels at the level of symmetry.

\section{2. Impossibility in the minimal vector mediator}

Consider a single vector mediator with interaction
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}=g_X X_i^a \mathbb J_i^a.
}
\end{equation}

If \(\mathbb J_i^a\) is the most general linear combination
\[
\mathbb J_i^a = c_\parallel J_i^a + c_E \mathcal E_i^a + c_O \mathcal O_i^a,
\]
then, without additional structure, all coefficients are equally allowed.

Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_\parallel,\;c_E,\;c_O \sim O(1)
\quad \text{(natural expectation).}
}
\label{eq:sel_O1}
\end{equation}

So:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{longitudinal-only selection is impossible in the minimal vector mediator.}
}
\label{eq:sel_impossible_minimal}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Possible mechanisms for suppression}

To achieve
\[
c_E,c_O \ll c_\parallel,
\]
one must introduce additional structure beyond the minimal setup.

We classify all possible mechanisms.

\subsection{(A) Internal symmetry selection}

If there exists an internal operator \(Q\) such that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Q(J_i^a)=+J_i^a,
\qquad
Q(\mathcal E_i^a)=-\mathcal E_i^a,
\qquad
Q(\mathcal O_i^a)=-\mathcal O_i^a,
}
\end{equation}
and the mediator transforms as
\[
Q(X_i^a)=+X_i^a,
\]
then the interaction
\[
X_i^a \mathbb J_i^a
\]
forces
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_E=c_O=0.
}
\end{equation}

This is a clean symmetry-based realization.

\subsection{(B) Multi-mediator structure}

Introduce two mediators:
\[
X_i^a \quad \text{and} \quad Y_i^a,
\]
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X_i^a \to J_i^a,
\qquad
Y_i^a \to \mathcal E_i^a,\mathcal O_i^a.
}
\end{equation}

If \(Y\) is heavy or weakly coupled,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\gamma_E,\gamma_O \ll \alpha_X.
}
\end{equation}

This generates an effective hierarchy.

\subsection{(C) Derivative suppression}

If \(\mathcal E,\mathcal O\) involve extra derivatives compared to \(J\),
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_E,c_O \sim \frac{k}{\Lambda} c_\parallel,
}
\end{equation}
leading to suppression in the IR.

However, in the current construction, all channels are first-order,
so this mechanism is not automatically available.

\subsection{(D) Representation mismatch}

If \(J\) and \(\mathcal E,\mathcal O\) belong to different irreducible representations of an enlarged symmetry group,
then a mediator in a specific representation can couple only to one sector.

This requires extending the symmetry beyond the minimal SO(3) structure.

\section{4. Necessary condition for natural isotropy}

From the anisotropy condition,
\[
|\rho|\sim 1
\quad \Longrightarrow \quad
\frac{|c_\parallel|}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
\sim
\frac{32}{3}\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2},
\]
we see that
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{natural isotropy requires a symmetry or mechanism enforcing }
c_E,c_O \ll c_\parallel.
}
\label{eq:sel_iso_req}
\end{equation}

Without such a mechanism, isotropy is not natural.

\section{5. Honest classification}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\begin{array}{lcl}
\text{Minimal vector mediator} &:& \text{impossible} \\
\text{Internal symmetry selection} &:& \text{possible} \\
\text{Multi-mediator hierarchy} &:& \text{possible} \\
\text{Derivative suppression} &:& \text{not generic here} \\
\text{Representation engineering} &:& \text{possible}
\end{array}
}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Final structural conclusion}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{The minimal TECT branch predicts anisotropic Dirac cones.}
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Isotropic Dirac requires an additional microscopic selection rule.}
}
\end{equation}
