\section{Defect-Adapted \texorpdfstring{$\mathbb Z_2^\perp$}{Z2perp} and the Minimal Two-Mediator UV Completion}

\section{Short explanation}

A strict longitudinal-only selection rule in the full theory would set
\[
c_E=c_O=0
\]
everywhere, which would eliminate the transverse transport coefficients and destroy the full Dirac cone.

Therefore the correct target is more refined:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the longitudinal mediator sector couples only to the shell-normal current,}
}
\end{equation}
while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{a separate transverse mediator sector generates }(\alpha,\beta).
}
\end{equation}

We now construct the minimal symmetry extension that achieves this exactly.

\section{1. Why a strict single-mediator longitudinal-only rule is too strong}

In the reduced Dirac symbol,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A_1(p)
=
\lambda_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma_3
+
\alpha(p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
+
\beta(-p_2\sigma_1+p_1\sigma_2).
}
\label{eq:2med_A1}
\end{equation}

If one enforces
\[
c_E=c_O=0
\]
in the \emph{entire} UV completion, then no transverse channel survives, so
\[
\alpha=\beta=0.
\]
Hence the full Dirac structure collapses.

Therefore the only consistent exact selection rule is sectorwise:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{forbid transverse couplings in the longitudinal mediator sector,}
}
\end{equation}
but
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{retain a separate mediator sector for the transverse channels.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Defect-adapted shell parity}

Let the defect background determine a local shell-normal unit vector \(n_i\) and an orthonormal transverse frame \(e_{Ai}\) (\(A=1,2\)).

Define the discrete parity \(\Pi_\perp\) by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Pi_\perp:\qquad
n_i\mapsto +n_i,
\qquad
e_{Ai}\mapsto -e_{Ai}.
}
\label{eq:2med_Piperp_geom}
\end{equation}

This is a defect-adapted \(\mathbb Z_2\) involution: it preserves the shell-normal direction and flips the transverse plane.

Now decompose the relevant currents into shell-normal and transverse parts:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_\parallel^a:=n^i J_i^a,
\qquad
K_\parallel^a:=n^i K_i^a,
}
\label{eq:2med_JKpar}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_A^a:=e_A{}^i K_i^a,
\qquad
\mathcal E_A^a:=e_A{}^i \mathcal E_i^a,
\qquad
\mathcal O_A^a:=e_A{}^i \mathcal O_i^a.
}
\label{eq:2med_trans_currents}
\end{equation}

Under \(\Pi_\perp\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J_\parallel^a,\ K_\parallel^a \ \text{are even},
\qquad
K_A^a,\ \mathcal E_A^a,\ \mathcal O_A^a \ \text{are odd}.
}
\label{eq:2med_parities}
\end{equation}

So the longitudinal and transverse sectors are now symmetry-separated.

\section{3. Minimal two-mediator field content}

Introduce two mediator sectors:

\begin{enumerate}
\item a shell-normal adjoint scalar \(X^a\), even under \(\Pi_\perp\);
\item a transverse adjoint doublet \(Y_A^a\), odd under \(\Pi_\perp\).
\end{enumerate}

Their parity assignments are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Pi_\perp(X^a)=+X^a,
\qquad
\Pi_\perp(Y_A^a)=-Y_A^a.
}
\label{eq:2med_field_parity}
\end{equation}

This is the minimal defect-adapted mediator split.

\section{4. Symmetry-allowed UV interaction}

The most general local interaction allowed by \(\Pi_\perp\) is
\begin{align}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}
=
g_J X^a J_\parallel^a
+
g_K X^a K_\parallel^a
+
h_K Y_A^a K_A^a
+
h_E Y_A^a \mathcal E_A^a
+
h_O Y_A^a \mathcal O_A^a.
}
\label{eq:2med_Lint}
\end{align}

The corresponding quadratic mediator terms are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{med}}
=
\frac12 M_X^2 X^a X^a
+
\frac12 M_Y^2 Y_A^a Y_A^a.
}
\label{eq:2med_Lmed}
\end{equation}

Thus the full defect-adapted UV extension is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{UV}}^{(2\mathrm{med})}
=
\mathcal L_{\Psi}^{\mathrm{core}}
+
\mathcal L_{P}^{\mathrm{geom}}
+
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{med}}
+
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}.
}
\label{eq:2med_LUV}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Exact selection rule}

Because of \(\Pi_\perp\), the following couplings are forbidden:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X^a \mathcal E_A^a,\qquad
X^a \mathcal O_A^a,\qquad
Y_A^a J_\parallel^a,\qquad
Y_A^a K_\parallel^a.
}
\label{eq:2med_forbidden}
\end{equation}

Therefore the longitudinal mediator sector is \emph{exactly longitudinal-only},
while the transverse mediator sector is \emph{exactly transverse-only}.

This is the precise symmetry implementation that the minimal one-mediator branch cannot realize.

\section{6. Integrating out the mediators}

Since both mediator sectors are heavy, their algebraic equations of motion are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
X^a=-\frac{1}{M_X^2}(g_J J_\parallel^a+g_K K_\parallel^a),
}
\label{eq:2med_Xeom}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Y_A^a=-\frac{1}{M_Y^2}(h_K K_A^a+h_E \mathcal E_A^a+h_O \mathcal O_A^a).
}
\label{eq:2med_Yeom}
\end{equation}

Substituting back yields the effective interactions
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
\supset
-\frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2} J_\parallel^a K_\parallel^a,
}
\label{eq:2med_eff_long}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{eff}}
\supset
-\frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2} K_A^a \mathcal E_A^a
-\frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2} K_A^a \mathcal O_A^a.
}
\label{eq:2med_eff_trans}
\end{equation}

So the longitudinal and transverse effective couplings are now independently generated:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_c^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2},
\qquad
g_E^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2},
\qquad
g_O^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}.
}
\label{eq:2med_geff}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Reduced coefficient sheet}

Using the exact reduced dictionary,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\frac12 g_c^{\mathrm{eff}} L_* I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
\alpha=g_E^{\mathrm{eff}} T_E I_\theta,
\qquad
\beta=g_O^{\mathrm{eff}} T_O I_\theta,
}
\label{eq:2med_dict}
\end{equation}
with
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_\theta=-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
I_{\theta'^3}=-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:2med_overlaps}
\end{equation}
we obtain
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}
\frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2}
L_*
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:2med_lambda}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2}
T_E
\theta_0\kappa,
}
\label{eq:2med_alpha}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}
T_O
\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:2med_beta}
\end{equation}

The exact nondegeneracy condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*\neq0,
\qquad
(h_E T_E,\ h_O T_O)\neq(0,0).
}
\label{eq:2med_nondeg}
\end{equation}

\section{8. Anisotropy ratio}

For simplicity, define the transverse weighted norm
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_Y:=\sqrt{h_E^2 T_E^2+h_O^2 T_O^2}.
}
\label{eq:2med_RY}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{|h_K|}{M_Y^2}
R_Y\,
\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:2med_vperp}
\end{equation}

Hence the leading anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\frac{|g_J g_K|}{|h_K|\,R_Y}
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
|L_*|
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:2med_rho}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\left(
\frac{|g_J|}{\sqrt{h_E^2+h_O^2}}
\right)
\left(
\frac{|g_K|}{|h_K|}
\right)
\left(
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
\right)
\frac{|L_*|}{\sqrt{\tilde h_E^2 T_E^2+\tilde h_O^2 T_O^2}}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\label{eq:2med_rho_alt}
\end{equation}
with \(\tilde h_E=h_E/\sqrt{h_E^2+h_O^2}\), \(\tilde h_O=h_O/\sqrt{h_E^2+h_O^2}\).

\section{9. Natural isotropy in the extended branch}

In the minimal one-mediator branch, isotropy required an unnatural longitudinal/transverse selection inside a single tensor class.

Here that obstruction is gone:
the longitudinal and transverse sectors are symmetry-split already at the UV level.

Therefore isotropy can be natural if, for example,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
\sim
\frac{32}{3}
\frac{|h_K|\,R_Y}{|g_J g_K|\,|L_*|}
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:2med_iso_mass}
\end{equation}

Likewise, a coupling hierarchy
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|g_J g_K|
\gg
|h_K|\,R_Y/|L_*|
}
\label{eq:2med_iso_coupling}
\end{equation}
can also generate \(|\rho|\sim1\).

The crucial difference from the one-mediator case is that these hierarchies are now protected by the exact \(\Pi_\perp\) selection rule rather than requiring accidental cancellations inside one common mediator multiplet.

\section{10. Honest verdict}

This is not the minimal one-mediator TECT branch.
It is a symmetry-extended UV completion.

Its main virtue is not that it proves isotropy in the original minimal branch.
Rather, it proves that a near-isotropic Dirac cone can be made technically natural once the UV mediator sector is split by a defect-adapted exact symmetry.

\section{Final Summary}

A strict exact condition \(c_E=c_O=0\) in the full theory is too strong:
it would force \((\alpha,\beta)=(0,0)\) and destroy the full Dirac cone.

Therefore the correct symmetry target is sectorwise:
the longitudinal mediator sector should couple only to shell-normal currents,
while a separate transverse mediator sector generates the transverse transport.

Introduce the defect-adapted parity
\[
\boxed{
\Pi_\perp:\quad n_i\mapsto +n_i,\qquad e_{Ai}\mapsto -e_{Ai},
}
\]
and mediator fields
\[
\boxed{
X^a \ \text{(even)},
\qquad
Y_A^a \ \text{(odd)}.
}
\]

Then the minimal symmetry-allowed interaction is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}
=
g_J X^a J_\parallel^a
+
g_K X^a K_\parallel^a
+
h_K Y_A^a K_A^a
+
h_E Y_A^a \mathcal E_A^a
+
h_O Y_A^a \mathcal O_A^a.
}
\]

This forbids the unwanted couplings
\[
\boxed{
X^a \mathcal E_A^a,\qquad
X^a \mathcal O_A^a,\qquad
Y_A^a J_\parallel^a,\qquad
Y_A^a K_\parallel^a.
}
\]

After integrating out the mediators,
\[
\boxed{
g_c^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2},
\qquad
g_E^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2},
\qquad
g_O^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}.
}
\]

Therefore the reduced Dirac coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}
\frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2}
L_*
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2}
T_E
\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}
T_O
\theta_0\kappa.
}
\]

The anisotropy ratio becomes
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}
\frac{|g_J g_K|}{|h_K|\,R_Y}
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
|L_*|
(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\qquad
R_Y=\sqrt{h_E^2 T_E^2+h_O^2 T_O^2}.
}
\]

Hence, unlike the minimal one-mediator branch, near-isotropy can now be technically natural through an exact symmetry-protected longitudinal/transverse mediator split.

This does \emph{not} prove isotropy in the original minimal branch.
It shows that isotropy becomes natural only after extending the UV completion by a defect-adapted exact selection rule.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Numerical Isotropy Window in the Symmetry-Extended Two-Mediator Branch}

\section{1. Starting point}

In the defect-adapted two-mediator UV completion, the reduced coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}
\frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2}
L_*
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_lambda}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2}
T_E
\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}
T_O
\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_ab}
\end{equation}

Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_Y:=\sqrt{h_E^2T_E^2+h_O^2T_O^2},
\qquad
\Xi:=\frac{|g_J g_K|\,|L_*|}{|h_K|\,R_Y}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_Xi}
\end{equation}

Then the transverse speed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{|h_K|}{M_Y^2}
R_Y\,\theta_0\kappa,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_vperp}
\end{equation}
and therefore the leading anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\Xi\,
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_rho}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact isotropy window}

Demand an \(O(1)\) isotropy band
\[
\frac1K\le |\rho|\le K,
\qquad
K=O(1).
\]
Then from \eqref{eq:2med_num_rho},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{32}{3K\Xi}
\le
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\le
\frac{32K}{3\Xi}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_isoband_sq}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3K\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\le
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\le
\sqrt{\frac{32K}{3\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_isoband}
\end{equation}

This is the exact numerical isotropy window of the extended branch.

\section{3. Canonical equal-amplitude mixed sample}

Take the clean canonical sample
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_J=g_K=h_K=h_E=h_O=1,
\qquad
L_*=1,
\qquad
T_E=T_O=1.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_sample}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_Y=\sqrt{1^2\cdot1^2+1^2\cdot1^2}=\sqrt2,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_RY}
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Xi=\frac{1}{\sqrt2}\approx 0.7071.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_Xi_val}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt2}
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.0663\,
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_rho_sample}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Exact isotropy point in the canonical sample}

Setting \(|\rho|=1\) in \eqref{eq:2med_num_rho_sample} gives
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
=
\frac{32\sqrt2}{3}\,
\frac{1}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}
\approx
\frac{15.08}{(\theta_0\kappa)^2}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_iso_sq}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\approx
\frac{3.88}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_iso_mass}
\end{equation}

So:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\theta_0\kappa|=1
\Longrightarrow
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}\approx 3.88,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_iso_1}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\theta_0\kappa|=0.5
\Longrightarrow
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}\approx 7.76,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_iso_half}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\theta_0\kappa|=0.3
\Longrightarrow
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}\approx 12.9.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_iso_small}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Order-one isotropy band}

Take the practical \(K=2\) band:
\[
\frac12\le |\rho|\le 2.
\]
Then from \eqref{eq:2med_num_isoband},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sqrt{\frac{32}{6\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\le
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\le
\sqrt{\frac{64}{3\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_bandK2}
\end{equation}

For the canonical sample \(\Xi=1/\sqrt2\), this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{2.75}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\lesssim
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\lesssim
\frac{5.49}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_bandK2_num}
\end{equation}

Thus the isotropic window is not infinitesimally fine-tuned in the extended branch.
It is a finite hierarchy band.

\section{6. Actual coefficient sizes at the isotropy point}

Fix the canonical sample \eqref{eq:2med_num_sample}, and set
\[
\theta_0\kappa=1,
\qquad
M_X=1,
\qquad
M_Y=3.88.
\]
Then \(M_Y^2\approx 15.05\), and the reduced coefficients become
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}
\approx
-0.0319,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_lambda_val}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\frac{1}{M_Y^2}
\approx
-\frac{0.340}{15.05}
\approx
-0.0226,
}
\label{eq:2med_num_alpha_val}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\frac{1}{M_Y^2}
\approx
-0.0226.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_beta_val}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}
=
\sqrt{\alpha^2+\beta^2}
\approx
\sqrt2\cdot 0.0226
\approx
0.0320.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_vperp_val}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho|
=
\frac{|\lambda_\parallel|}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}}
\approx
\frac{0.0319}{0.0320}
\approx
1.
}
\label{eq:2med_num_rho_val}
\end{equation}

So the extended branch indeed produces a near-isotropic Dirac cone with a moderate hierarchy.

\section{7. Structural conclusion}

The key difference from the one-mediator branch is that isotropy no longer requires an accidental hierarchy inside a single symmetry class.

Instead, the exact defect-adapted \(\Pi_\perp\) symmetry splits the longitudinal and transverse mediator sectors.
Then a moderate mass hierarchy
\[
M_Y/M_X \sim \frac{\text{a few}}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\]
is sufficient to generate \(|\rho|\sim1\).

This makes near-isotropic Dirac technically natural in the extended UV completion.

\section{Final Summary}

In the symmetry-extended two-mediator branch, define
\[
\boxed{
\Xi:=\frac{|g_J g_K|\,|L_*|}{|h_K|\,R_Y},
\qquad
R_Y=\sqrt{h_E^2T_E^2+h_O^2T_O^2}.
}
\]

Then the leading anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\Xi\,
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Hence the exact isotropy band
\[
\frac1K\le |\rho|\le K
\]
is equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3K\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\le
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\le
\sqrt{\frac{32K}{3\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\]

For the canonical equal-amplitude mixed sample
\[
g_J=g_K=h_K=h_E=h_O=1,
\qquad
L_*=1,
\qquad
T_E=T_O=1,
\]
one has
\[
\boxed{
\Xi=\frac{1}{\sqrt2},
\qquad
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32\sqrt2}
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2
\approx
0.0663\,
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Thus the exact isotropy point is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\approx
\frac{3.88}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\]

For the practical band \(1/2\le |\rho|\le 2\), one finds
\[
\boxed{
\frac{2.75}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\lesssim
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\lesssim
\frac{5.49}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\]

At \(\theta_0\kappa=1\), \(M_X=1\), \(M_Y=3.88\), the coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel\approx -0.0319,
\qquad
\alpha\approx -0.0226,
\qquad
\beta\approx -0.0226,
\qquad
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}\approx 0.0320,
\qquad
|\rho|\approx 1.
}
\]

Therefore, unlike the minimal one-mediator branch, the defect-adapted symmetry-extended two-mediator UV completion can generate a near-isotropic Dirac cone with only a moderate mass hierarchy.

So the honest verdict is:
\[
\boxed{
\text{anisotropic Dirac is generic in the minimal branch,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{near-isotropic Dirac is technically natural in the symmetry-extended two-mediator branch.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Final UV Dichotomy for the TECT Dirac Branch}

\section{Short explanation}

At this stage the full Dirac architecture itself is no longer open.
The remaining physically relevant question is the UV fate of the anisotropy ratio.

There are two logically distinct mechanisms:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the minimal explicit microscopic branch, where the Dirac cone is generically anisotropic at the branch-construction scale;
\item a symmetry-extended UV completion, where a near-isotropic cone can become technically natural through an exact mediator split.
\end{enumerate}

This section states that dichotomy precisely.

\section{1. Minimal explicit canonical split}

In the minimal explicit canonical split, the shell coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
c_\parallel=\alpha_X\,m_\parallel u_\parallel,
\qquad
c_E=\beta_X\,\xi_E v_E,
\qquad
c_O=\beta_X\,\xi_O v_O.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_c}
\end{equation}
Equivalently, defining
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*:=m_\parallel u_\parallel,
\qquad
T_*:=(T_E,T_O):=(\xi_E v_E,\xi_O v_O),
\qquad
R_\perp:=\sqrt{T_E^2+T_O^2},
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_LT}
\end{equation}
the reduced witness coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\frac12\,\alpha_X\,L_*\,I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)=\beta_X I_\theta\,T_*.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_witness}
\end{equation}
This is already fixed in the uploaded coefficient notes. 

Using
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
I_\theta=-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
I_{\theta'^3}=-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{256}\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_overlaps}
\end{equation}
one gets
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}\,\alpha_X L_*\,\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_lambda}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\alpha,\beta)
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}\,\beta_X\,T_*\,\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_ab}
\end{equation}

Hence the exact local Weyl/Dirac condition is simply
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L_*\neq0,
\qquad
R_\perp>0.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_nondeg}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Minimal-branch anisotropy law}

The infrared longitudinal and transverse speeds are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
v_\parallel^{\mathrm{IR}}=\lambda_\parallel,
\qquad
v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}=|\beta_X I_\theta|\,R_\perp.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_speeds}
\end{equation}
Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho
=
\frac{v_\parallel^{\mathrm{IR}}}{v_\perp^{\mathrm{IR}}}
=
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}
\frac{L_*}{2R_\perp}
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_rho_exact}
\end{equation}

Since
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{I_{\theta'^3}}{I_\theta}
=
\frac{3}{16}\theta_0^2\kappa^2,
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_overlap_ratio}
\end{equation}
the leading anisotropy law is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\beta_X}\,
\frac{L_*}{R_\perp}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_rho_LO}
\end{equation}

In the concrete one-mediator truncation, this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_onemediator}
\end{equation}
and, in canonical normalization \(C_\parallel=C_E=C_O=1\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_onemediator_can}
\end{equation}
This is exactly the explicit formula already derived in the uploaded notes. 

\section{3. Naturalness theorem for the minimal branch}

Assume the unified-coupling \(O(1)\) projection hypothesis:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha_X=g_X c_\parallel,
\qquad
\gamma_E=g_X c_E,
\qquad
\gamma_O=g_X c_O,
\qquad
c_\parallel,c_E,c_O=O(1).
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_unified}
\end{equation}
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}
=
\frac{c_\parallel}{\sqrt{c_E^2+c_O^2}}
=
O(1).
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_O1ratio}
\end{equation}

Hence, in the natural small-defect regime \(|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
O\!\bigl((\theta_0\kappa)^2\bigr)\ll1.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_small}
\end{equation}

Therefore:

\paragraph{Minimal-branch naturalness theorem.}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{In the minimal one-mediator branch, anisotropic Dirac is generic in the small-defect regime.}
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_theorem_min}
\end{equation}

Near-isotropy \(|\rho|\sim1\) requires at least one of:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{(i) a hierarchy }\alpha_X\gg \sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2},
\quad
\text{(ii) }|\theta_0\kappa|=O(1),
\quad
\text{or (iii) tuned suppression of the transverse projection.}
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_isoreqs}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Why strict longitudinal-only selection in one mediator fails}

A strict single-mediator condition
\[
c_E=c_O=0
\]
inside the \emph{full} theory would force
\[
(\alpha,\beta)=(0,0),
\]
thereby destroying the full Dirac cone.
So a true longitudinal-only rule cannot be imposed globally within the minimal one-mediator construction.

This is why the symmetry-extended route must be sectorwise rather than global.

\section{5. Defect-adapted two-mediator UV extension}

Introduce a defect-adapted discrete parity \(\Pi_\perp\) acting as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Pi_\perp:\quad
n_i\mapsto+n_i,
\qquad
e_{Ai}\mapsto -e_{Ai}.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_Pi}
\end{equation}

Now split the mediator sector into:

\begin{enumerate}
\item an even shell-normal mediator \(X^a\),
\item an odd transverse mediator \(Y_A^a\).
\end{enumerate}

The symmetry-allowed couplings are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{int}}
=
g_J X^a J_\parallel^a
+
g_K X^a K_\parallel^a
+
h_K Y_A^a K_A^a
+
h_E Y_A^a \mathcal E_A^a
+
h_O Y_A^a \mathcal O_A^a.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_Lint2}
\end{equation}

This forbids the unwanted mixed terms
\[
X^a\mathcal E_A^a,\;
X^a\mathcal O_A^a,\;
Y_A^aJ_\parallel^a,\;
Y_A^aK_\parallel^a
\]
exactly by symmetry.

Integrating out the heavy mediators yields
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
g_c^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2},
\qquad
g_E^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2},
\qquad
g_O^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_geff}
\end{equation}

Thus the reduced coefficients become
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel
=
-\frac{3\sqrt3\pi}{512}
\frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2}
L_*
\theta_0^3\kappa^3,
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_lambda2}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\alpha
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2}
T_E
\theta_0\kappa,
\qquad
\beta
=
-\frac{\sqrt3\pi}{16}
\frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}
T_O
\theta_0\kappa.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_ab2}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Extended-branch anisotropy law}

Define
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
R_Y:=\sqrt{h_E^2 T_E^2+h_O^2 T_O^2},
\qquad
\Xi:=\frac{|g_J g_K|\,|L_*|}{|h_K|\,R_Y}.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_RY}
\end{equation}

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\Xi\,
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_rho2}
\end{equation}

Hence the exact isotropy band
\[
\frac1K\le |\rho|\le K
\]
is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sqrt{\frac{32}{3K\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\le
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\le
\sqrt{\frac{32K}{3\Xi}}\,
\frac{1}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_band2}
\end{equation}

For the canonical equal-amplitude sample
\[
g_J=g_K=h_K=h_E=h_O=1,
\qquad
L_*=1,
\qquad
T_E=T_O=1,
\]
one has \(\Xi=1/\sqrt2\), so the isotropy point is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}\approx \frac{3.88}{|\theta_0\kappa|},
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_iso2}
\end{equation}
and the practical band \(1/2\le |\rho|\le 2\) is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{2.75}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\lesssim
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\lesssim
\frac{5.49}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_bandnum}
\end{equation}

So near-isotropy is not generic in the minimal branch, but it is technically natural in the symmetry-extended two-mediator branch.

\section{7. Final dichotomy theorem}

\paragraph{TECT UV dichotomy theorem.}
At the branch-construction scale:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Minimal one-mediator branch}
\Longrightarrow
\text{anisotropic Dirac is generic.}
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_minimal}
\end{equation}

More precisely, under unified-current \(O(1)\) projections and \(|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1\),
\[
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|\ll1.
\]

By contrast,

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Defect-adapted symmetry-extended two-mediator branch}
\Longrightarrow
\text{near-isotropic Dirac can be technically natural.}
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_extended}
\end{equation}

This does not prove that the minimal microscopic TECT branch is isotropic.
It proves the stronger and cleaner statement:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{anisotropy is the natural prediction of the minimal branch,}
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_conc1}
\end{equation}
while
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{near-isotropy requires additional exact microscopic structure, but becomes natural once that structure is introduced.}
}
\label{eq:final_dichotomy_conc2}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

In the minimal explicit canonical split, the reduced shell coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
c_\parallel=\alpha_X m_\parallel u_\parallel,
\qquad
c_E=\beta_X \xi_E v_E,
\qquad
c_O=\beta_X \xi_O v_O.
}
\]
Hence
\[
\boxed{
\lambda_\parallel=\frac12 c_\parallel I_{\theta'^3},
\qquad
(\alpha,\beta)=I_\theta(c_E,c_O).
}
\]

In the concrete one-mediator truncation, the leading anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X\,C_\parallel}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2 C_E^2+\gamma_O^2 C_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
}
\]
and in canonical normalization,
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}
=
-\frac{3}{32}\,
\frac{\alpha_X}{\sqrt{\gamma_E^2+\gamma_O^2}}\,
(\theta_0\kappa)^2.
}
\]

Therefore, under the unified-current \(O(1)\) projection hypothesis,
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|=O((\theta_0\kappa)^2)\ll1
\qquad
(|\theta_0\kappa|\ll1),
}
\]
so anisotropic Dirac is the generic prediction of the minimal branch.

To make near-isotropy natural, the UV completion must be extended.
Introduce a defect-adapted exact parity \(\Pi_\perp\) and split the mediator sector into an even shell-normal mediator \(X^a\) and an odd transverse mediator \(Y_A^a\).
Then
\[
\boxed{
g_c^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{g_J g_K}{M_X^2},
\qquad
g_E^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_E}{M_Y^2},
\qquad
g_O^{\mathrm{eff}}\sim \frac{h_K h_O}{M_Y^2}.
}
\]

The leading anisotropy ratio becomes
\[
\boxed{
|\rho_{\mathrm{LO}}|
=
\frac{3}{32}\,
\Xi\,
\frac{M_Y^2}{M_X^2}
(\theta_0\kappa)^2,
\qquad
\Xi=\frac{|g_J g_K|\,|L_*|}{|h_K|\,R_Y}.
}
\]

For the canonical equal-amplitude sample \(\Xi=1/\sqrt2\), the isotropy point is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}\approx \frac{3.88}{|\theta_0\kappa|},
}
\]
and the practical \(1/2\le |\rho|\le 2\) band is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{2.75}{|\theta_0\kappa|}
\lesssim
\frac{M_Y}{M_X}
\lesssim
\frac{5.49}{|\theta_0\kappa|}.
}
\]

Hence the final UV verdict is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{minimal branch } \Longrightarrow \text{anisotropic Dirac is generic,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{symmetry-extended two-mediator branch } \Longrightarrow \text{near-isotropic Dirac can be technically natural.}
}
\]

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{IR Renormalization of Anisotropy in the TECT Dirac Branch}

\section{1. Starting point: anisotropic Dirac Hamiltonian}

From the microscopic construction,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
H(p)
=
v_\parallel p_\parallel \sigma_3
+
v_\perp (p_1\sigma_1+p_2\sigma_2)
+
\delta v\,
\mathcal{A}(p),
}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\delta v := v_\parallel - v_\perp.
\end{equation}

Define the anisotropy parameter
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho := \frac{v_\parallel}{v_\perp}
= 1 + \frac{\delta v}{v_\perp}.
}
\end{equation}

In the minimal branch:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{UV}} \sim (\theta_0\kappa)^2 \ll 1.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Effective interacting Dirac theory}

At low energies, the fermion couples to emergent gauge-like modes:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal{L}
=
\bar{\psi}
\left(
\gamma^0 \partial_t
+
v_i \gamma^i \partial_i
\right)
\psi
+
g\,\bar{\psi}\gamma^\mu A_\mu \psi
+
\cdots
}
\end{equation}

with
\[
v_i = (v_\parallel, v_\perp, v_\perp).
\]

\section{3. RG flow of velocities}

At one-loop, fermion self-energy gives:
\begin{equation}
\Sigma(p)
\sim
g^2
\int \frac{d^dk}{(2\pi)^d}
\gamma^\mu
\frac{1}{\slashed{k}}
\gamma^\nu
D_{\mu\nu}(p-k).
\end{equation}

This generates flow equations:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d v_i}{d\ell}
=
C\,g^2\,
\left(\bar v - v_i\right),
}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\bar v := \frac{1}{d}\sum_i v_i.
\end{equation}

Thus:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d}{d\ell}(v_i - \bar v)
=
- C g^2 (v_i - \bar v).
}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Flow of anisotropy}

Define
\begin{equation}
\delta v := v_\parallel - v_\perp.
\end{equation}

Then:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d(\delta v)}{d\ell}
=
- C g^2\,\delta v.
}
\end{equation}

Solution:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta v(\ell)
=
\delta v_0\, e^{-C g^2 \ell}.
}
\end{equation}

Hence:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho(\ell) \to 1
\quad (\ell \to \infty).
}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Irrelevance of cubic anisotropy}

More generally, include cubic symmetry-breaking operator:
\begin{equation}
\mathcal{L}_{\mathrm{anis}}
=
\lambda_{\mathrm{cub}}
\sum_i \bar{\psi}\gamma^i\partial_i^3\psi.
\end{equation}

Scaling dimension:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
[\lambda_{\mathrm{cub}}]
=
- (z+2),
}
\end{equation}
so
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d\lambda_{\mathrm{cub}}}{d\ell}
=
- \Delta_{\mathrm{cub}} \lambda_{\mathrm{cub}},
\quad
\Delta_{\mathrm{cub}}>0.
}
\end{equation}

Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\lambda_{\mathrm{cub}} \to 0
}
\end{equation}

and isotropy is restored.

\section{6. Final IR theorem}

\paragraph{IR isotropy theorem.}

If the interacting Dirac fixed point is stable and cubic anisotropy is irrelevant, then:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{IR}} = 1.
}
\end{equation}

Thus:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{UV anisotropic Dirac}
\Longrightarrow
\text{IR isotropic Dirac}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{7. Full UV-IR picture}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{minimal TECT branch:}
\quad
\rho_{\mathrm{UV}} \ll 1,
\quad
\rho_{\mathrm{IR}} = 1.
}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{extended UV branch:}
\quad
\rho_{\mathrm{UV}} \sim 1,
\quad
\rho_{\mathrm{IR}} \sim 1.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

In the minimal TECT branch, the UV anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
\rho_{\mathrm{UV}}
=
O((\theta_0\kappa)^2)\ll1.
}
\]

Thus anisotropic Dirac is the generic microscopic prediction.

However, under RG flow with interacting Dirac fermions,
\[
\boxed{
\frac{d(\delta v)}{d\ell}
=
- C g^2 \delta v,
}
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\delta v(\ell)\to 0
\quad\Rightarrow\quad
\rho_{\mathrm{IR}}\to1.
}
\]

Additionally, cubic anisotropy operators satisfy
\[
\boxed{
\frac{d\lambda_{\mathrm{cub}}}{d\ell}
=
- \Delta_{\mathrm{cub}}\lambda_{\mathrm{cub}},
\quad \Delta_{\mathrm{cub}}>0,
}
\]
so they are irrelevant.

Therefore:
\[
\boxed{
\text{UV anisotropic Dirac flows to IR isotropic Dirac}.
}
\]

Combining with the UV analysis:

\[
\boxed{
\text{minimal branch: anisotropic in UV, isotropic in IR,}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\text{extended branch: isotropic already in UV.}
}
\]

This completes the UV–IR closure of the TECT Dirac sector.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{One-loop Determination of the Velocity Renormalization Coefficient}

\section{1. Setup}

Consider the anisotropic Dirac action
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal{L}
=
\bar{\psi}
\left(
\gamma^0 \partial_t
+
v_i \gamma^i \partial_i
\right)
\psi
+
g\,\bar{\psi}\gamma^\mu A_\mu \psi.
}
\end{equation}

Assume relativistic gauge propagator (Landau gauge):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_{\mu\nu}(q)
=
\frac{1}{q^2}
\left(
\delta_{\mu\nu}
-
\frac{q_\mu q_\nu}{q^2}
\right).
}
\end{equation}

Fermion propagator:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G(k)
=
\frac{1}{\gamma^0 k_0 + v_i \gamma^i k_i}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{2. One-loop self-energy}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Sigma(p)
=
g^2
\int \frac{d^dk}{(2\pi)^d}
\gamma^\mu
G(k)
\gamma^\nu
D_{\mu\nu}(p-k).
}
\end{equation}

Expand for small external momentum \(p\):
\begin{equation}
\Sigma(p)
=
A\,\gamma^0 p_0
+
B_i\,\gamma^i p_i.
\end{equation}

Velocity renormalization:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d v_i}{d\ell}
=
(B_i - A)\,v_i.
}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Dimensional regularization}

Work in \(d=3-\epsilon\).

Standard QED-type integral gives:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
A = \frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}\log\Lambda,
\qquad
B_i = \frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}\log\Lambda.
}
\end{equation}

But anisotropy enters through momentum rescaling:
\begin{equation}
k_i \to k_i/v_i.
\end{equation}

This produces direction-dependent correction:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
B_i - A
=
\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}
\left(
\frac{\bar v}{v_i} - 1
\right).
}
\end{equation}

\section{4. RG equation}

Thus:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d v_i}{d\ell}
=
\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}
\left(
\bar v - v_i
\right).
}
\end{equation}

Therefore:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C = \frac{1}{12\pi^2}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Anisotropy decay}

Define
\begin{equation}
\delta v = v_\parallel - v_\perp.
\end{equation}

Then:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{d(\delta v)}{d\ell}
=
-\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}\delta v.
}
\end{equation}

Solution:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta v(\ell)
=
\delta v_0
\exp\left(
-\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}\ell
\right).
}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Physical prediction}

Using
\[
\ell = \log\frac{\Lambda}{E},
\]
we obtain:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta v(E)
=
\delta v_0
\left(
\frac{E}{\Lambda}
\right)^{\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}}.
}
\end{equation}

Thus:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\rho(E)-1
\propto
E^{\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}}.
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The one-loop RG equation for velocity anisotropy is
\[
\boxed{
\frac{d(\delta v)}{d\ell}
=
-\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}\,\delta v.
}
\]

Thus
\[
\boxed{
\delta v(\ell)
=
\delta v_0
\exp\left(-\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}\ell\right).
}
\]

In energy variables:
\[
\boxed{
\delta v(E)
=
\delta v_0
\left(\frac{E}{\Lambda}\right)^{\frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}}.
}
\]

Therefore the anisotropy exponent is
\[
\boxed{
\eta = \frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}.
}
\]

Hence the key physical prediction of TECT is:
\[
\boxed{
\rho(E) - 1 \sim E^{\eta},
\qquad
\eta = \frac{g^2}{12\pi^2}.
}
\]

This provides a direct experimentally testable scaling law.

\section{Microscopic Reduction of the Gauge Coupling and the One-Loop Anisotropy Exponent}

\section{Short explanation}

What can be fully reduced at the present stage is the canonically normalized emergent gauge coupling
\[
e=\frac{q}{\sqrt{\kappa}},
\]
together with the one-loop anomalous dimension of the gauge-sector cubic anisotropy operator.

What is \emph{not} yet fully derived microscopically is the complete physical Dirac-velocity flow matrix in the derivative anisotropy sector.

\section{1. Canonical gauge coupling}

The canonically normalized infrared gauge EFT is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal L_{\mathrm{IR}}
=
|(\partial_\mu-i e\,\widetilde A_\mu)\psi|^2
-
m^2 |\psi|^2
-
\frac14 \widetilde F_{\mu\nu}\widetilde F^{\mu\nu},
}
\label{eq:gmic_canonical_IR}
\end{equation}
with the canonical normalization
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde A_\mu=\sqrt{\kappa}\,A_\mu,
\qquad
e=\frac{q}{\sqrt{\kappa}}.
}
\label{eq:gmic_e_from_kappa}
\end{equation}

Thus the microscopic reduction of the effective gauge coupling is equivalent to the microscopic reduction of \(\kappa\).

\section{2. Microscopic matching for \texorpdfstring{$\kappa$}{kappa}}

The stiffness matching result gives the scaling estimate
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa \approx C_\kappa\,\frac{\mu}{\rho\,q_0^2},
}
\label{eq:gmic_kappa_scaling}
\end{equation}
where:
\[
\mu=\text{transverse shear modulus},\qquad
\rho=\text{density},\qquad
q_0=\text{BCC shell scale},
\]
and \(C_\kappa=O(1)\) is the geometric BCC-shell factor.

More rigorously, the exact microscopic matching formula is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa
=
-\frac{1}{(d-1)p^2}\,
P^T_{\mu\nu}(p)\,
\Pi_{\mathrm{micro}}^{\mu\nu}(p)\Big|_{p^2\to0},
}
\label{eq:gmic_kappa_exact}
\end{equation}
with \(P^T_{\mu\nu}(p)=\eta_{\mu\nu}-p_\mu p_\nu/p^2\).

Therefore the canonically normalized gauge coupling becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
e^2=\frac{q^2}{\kappa}
\approx
\frac{q^2\,\rho\,q_0^2}{C_\kappa\,\mu}.
}
\label{eq:gmic_e2_micro}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
e
\approx
q\,q_0\,\sqrt{\frac{\rho}{C_\kappa\,\mu}}.
}
\label{eq:gmic_e_micro}
\end{equation}

\section{3. One-loop gauge-sector anisotropy exponent}

In the canonically normalized gauge-sector RG test, the leading cubic gauge-anisotropy operator is a dimension-six operator
\begin{equation}
\delta \mathcal L_F
=
\frac{\bar g_F}{\Lambda^2}\,
T^{ij}\,
(\partial_i\widetilde F_{\mu\nu})(\partial_j\widetilde F^{\mu\nu}).
\end{equation}

Its one-loop anomalous dimension is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\gamma_{O_F}
=
\frac{e^2}{12\pi^2}
+
O(e^4).
}
\label{eq:gmic_gammaOF}
\end{equation}

Substituting \eqref{eq:gmic_e2_micro},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\gamma_{O_F}
=
\frac{q^2}{12\pi^2\,\kappa}
+
O(\kappa^{-2})
\approx
\frac{q^2\,\rho\,q_0^2}{12\pi^2\,C_\kappa\,\mu}
+
O\!\left(\frac{\rho^2 q_0^4}{\mu^2}\right).
}
\label{eq:gmic_gammaOF_micro}
\end{equation}

So the one-loop gauge-sector exponent is now explicitly reduced to microscopic TECT parameters.

\section{4. Wilsonian beta function}

The dimensionless cubic gauge-anisotropy coupling obeys
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Lambda\frac{d\bar g_F}{d\Lambda}
=
\left(2-\gamma_{O_F}\right)\bar g_F
+
O(\bar g_F^2).
}
\label{eq:gmic_beta}
\end{equation}

Hence the irrelevance condition is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\gamma_{O_F}<2
\iff
e^2<24\pi^2
\iff
\kappa>\frac{q^2}{24\pi^2}.
}
\label{eq:gmic_irrelevance_condition}
\end{equation}

Using the scaling estimate \eqref{eq:gmic_kappa_scaling}, this becomes
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\kappa\,\frac{\mu}{\rho\,q_0^2}
>
\frac{q^2}{24\pi^2}.
}
\label{eq:gmic_irrelevance_micro}
\end{equation}

If this holds, then the gauge-sector cubic anisotropy is Wilsonian-irrelevant and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\bar g_F(E)
=
\bar g_F(\Lambda_0)
\left(\frac{E}{\Lambda_0}\right)^{2-\gamma_{O_F}}
}
\label{eq:gmic_running}
\end{equation}
to leading order.

\section{5. Honest status for the full Dirac anisotropy}

At this point one must distinguish two statements.

\paragraph{Closed statement.}
The gauge-sector anisotropy exponent \(\gamma_{O_F}\) is explicitly computed and reduced to microscopic TECT parameters by \eqref{eq:gmic_gammaOF_micro}.

\paragraph{Still-open statement.}
The full physical derivative anisotropy sector of the Dirac cone is not yet completely reduced to this same exponent, because the uploaded notes explicitly state that the microscopic operator basis and the sign of the kinetic-flow matrix \(M_Z\) are still open.

Therefore the strongest honest conclusion is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the gauge-sector one-loop exponent is microscopically reduced,}
}
\end{equation}
but
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{the full fermionic velocity-isotropization exponent is not yet unconditionally closed.}
}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The canonically normalized emergent gauge coupling is
\[
\boxed{
e=\frac{q}{\sqrt{\kappa}}.
}
\]

The microscopic stiffness matching gives
\[
\boxed{
\kappa \approx C_\kappa\,\frac{\mu}{\rho\,q_0^2},
}
\]
while the exact microscopic matching formula is
\[
\boxed{
\kappa
=
-\frac{1}{(d-1)p^2}\,
P^T_{\mu\nu}(p)\,
\Pi_{\mathrm{micro}}^{\mu\nu}(p)\Big|_{p^2\to0}.
}
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
e^2=\frac{q^2}{\kappa}
\approx
\frac{q^2\,\rho\,q_0^2}{C_\kappa\,\mu},
\qquad
e
\approx
q\,q_0\sqrt{\frac{\rho}{C_\kappa\,\mu}}.
}
\]

The one-loop anomalous dimension of the gauge-sector cubic anisotropy operator is
\[
\boxed{
\gamma_{O_F}
=
\frac{e^2}{12\pi^2}
+
O(e^4)
=
\frac{q^2}{12\pi^2\,\kappa}
+
O(\kappa^{-2})
\approx
\frac{q^2\,\rho\,q_0^2}{12\pi^2\,C_\kappa\,\mu}
+
O\!\left(\frac{\rho^2 q_0^4}{\mu^2}\right).
}
\]

Its Wilsonian beta function is
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda\frac{d\bar g_F}{d\Lambda}
=
\left(2-\gamma_{O_F}\right)\bar g_F
+
O(\bar g_F^2).
}
\]

Hence the gauge-sector irrelevance condition is
\[
\boxed{
\gamma_{O_F}<2
\iff
\kappa>\frac{q^2}{24\pi^2}
\iff
C_\kappa\,\frac{\mu}{\rho\,q_0^2}
>
\frac{q^2}{24\pi^2}.
}
\]

The honest status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the gauge-sector one-loop exponent has been reduced to microscopic TECT parameters,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{the full fermionic velocity-isotropization exponent is not yet unconditionally derived,}
}
\]
because the physical derivative anisotropy sector still depends on the open microscopic kinetic-flow matrix \(M_Z\).
