\section{Numerically Well-Posed TECT Flavor Program}

\section{Short explanation}

The correct next step is not to quote more toy overlap numbers.
It is to define a numerically identifiable minimal TECT flavor model whose outputs are the physical observables

\[
\{m_u,m_c,m_t,m_d,m_s,m_b, |V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|, J\},
\]

and then fit that model under explicit symmetry and regularity constraints.

At this stage, the mathematically proper task is to construct the minimal finite parameter family and the corresponding objective functional.

\section{1. Honest starting point}

The previously discussed Gaussian-overlap matrices should be treated only as leading-order illustrations.
They are not yet predictions because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the overlap ansatz was not written in an identifiable parameterization;
\item translational/rotational/rephasing redundancies were not fixed;
\item masses, CKM magnitudes, and CP violation were not fit simultaneously;
\item parameter sensitivity and uncertainty propagation were not analyzed.
\end{enumerate}

Therefore the first rigorous numerical task is to replace the toy overlap picture by a well-posed inverse problem.

\section{2. Minimal identifiable quark-sector ansatz}

Let \(Q_L^A\) (\(A=1,2,3\)) denote the three left-handed quark-family localized modes,
and let \(U_R^B,D_R^B\) denote the right-handed up/down localized modes.

We define the left-handed family wavefunctions as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_{Q,A}(x)
=
\mathcal N_Q\,
\exp\!\left[
-\frac12 (x-q_A)^T G_Q (x-q_A)
\right]
\exp\!\left[i\,\kappa_{Q,A}\!\cdot x + i\phi_{Q,A}\right],
}
\label{eq:numprog_left}
\end{equation}
and the right-handed sector wavefunctions as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_{U,B}(x)
=
\mathcal N_U\,
\exp\!\left[
-\frac12 (x-u_B)^T G_U (x-u_B)
\right]
\exp\!\left[i\,\kappa_{U,B}\!\cdot x + i\phi_{U,B}\right],
}
\label{eq:numprog_up}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_{D,B}(x)
=
\mathcal N_D\,
\exp\!\left[
-\frac12 (x-d_B)^T G_D (x-d_B)
\right]
\exp\!\left[i\,\kappa_{D,B}\!\cdot x + i\phi_{D,B}\right].
}
\label{eq:numprog_down}
\end{equation}

Here:

\begin{itemize}
\item \(q_A,u_B,d_B\in\mathbb R^3\) are localization centers;
\item \(G_Q,G_U,G_D\) are positive-definite \(3\times3\) localization matrices;
\item \(\kappa_{Q,A},\kappa_{U,B},\kappa_{D,B}\in\mathbb R^3\) encode shell-phase/momentum offsets;
\item \(\phi_{Q,A},\phi_{U,B},\phi_{D,B}\in\mathbb R\) are local phases.
\end{itemize}

This is the minimal anisotropic single-scale ansatz that can already produce:

\begin{enumerate}
\item hierarchical magnitudes through spatial separation;
\item anisotropic corrections through \(G_s\);
\item complex phases and CP violation through \(\kappa,\phi\).
\end{enumerate}

\section{3. Exact Gaussian overlap formula}

Assume that the effective Yukawa interaction in sector \(s\in\{u,d\}\) is locally weighted by a slowly varying condensate factor absorbed into a sector constant \(Y_0^{(s)}\).
Then the Yukawa matrices are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
Y_0^{(u)}
\int d^3x\,
\overline{\psi_{Q,A}(x)}\,
\psi_{U,B}(x),
}
\label{eq:numprog_Yu_def}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
Y_0^{(d)}
\int d^3x\,
\overline{\psi_{Q,A}(x)}\,
\psi_{D,B}(x).
}
\label{eq:numprog_Yd_def}
\end{equation}

If, in the first minimal stage, one sets
\[
G_Q=G_U=G_D=:G
\]
for analytic tractability, then the Gaussian integral is explicit:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_s)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_s
\exp\!\left[
-\frac14 \Delta x_{AB}^{(s)\,T} G\, \Delta x_{AB}^{(s)}
-\frac14 \Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(s)\,T} G^{-1}\Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(s)}
\right]
\exp\!\left[
i\,\Theta_{AB}^{(s)}
\right],
}
\label{eq:numprog_Y_exact}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta x_{AB}^{(u)}:=u_B-q_A,
\qquad
\Delta x_{AB}^{(d)}:=d_B-q_A,
}
\label{eq:numprog_Dx}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(u)}:=\kappa_{U,B}-\kappa_{Q,A},
\qquad
\Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(d)}:=\kappa_{D,B}-\kappa_{Q,A},
}
\label{eq:numprog_Dk}
\end{equation}
and
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta_{AB}^{(s)}
=
\frac12 \Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(s)}\cdot(x_{Q,A}+x_{s,B})
+
(\phi_{s,B}-\phi_{Q,A}),
}
\label{eq:numprog_phase}
\end{equation}
with \(\Lambda_s\) absorbing normalization factors and the overall sector Yukawa scale.

This is the first proper TECT Yukawa model:
it is explicit, finite-dimensional, and directly computable.

\section{4. Physical observables extracted from \(Y_u,Y_d\)}

Compute singular-value decompositions
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{uL}^\dagger Y_u U_{uR}
=
\mathrm{diag}(y_u,y_c,y_t),
\qquad
U_{dL}^\dagger Y_d U_{dR}
=
\mathrm{diag}(y_d,y_s,y_b),
}
\label{eq:numprog_svd}
\end{equation}
with positive singular values \(y_i\).

Then the quark masses are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_i^{(u)}=v_H\,y_i^{(u)},
\qquad
m_i^{(d)}=v_H\,y_i^{(d)},
}
\label{eq:numprog_masses}
\end{equation}
up to the appropriate condensate/Higgs normalization factor \(v_H\).

The CKM matrix is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_{uL}^\dagger U_{dL}.
}
\label{eq:numprog_ckm}
\end{equation}

The CP-violating invariant is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J
=
\mathrm{Im}
\left(
V_{ud}V_{cs}V_{us}^*V_{cd}^*
\right).
}
\label{eq:numprog_J}
\end{equation}

Thus the full observable vector is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{model}}
=
\{m_u,m_c,m_t,m_d,m_s,m_b,|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|,J\}.
}
\label{eq:numprog_obs}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Redundancy removal (identifiability)}

Before fitting, one must quotient out unphysical redundancies.

\subsection{5.1 Translation}
A common translation
\[
q_A,u_B,d_B\mapsto q_A+a,\ u_B+a,\ d_B+a
\]
leaves the magnitudes unchanged.
So we fix
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\sum_A q_A=0.
}
\label{eq:numprog_fix_translation}
\end{equation}

\subsection{5.2 Global rephasing}
Common family-independent phase shifts are unphysical.
Hence we fix one left and one right reference phase:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\phi_{Q,1}=0,\qquad \phi_{U,1}=0,\qquad \phi_{D,1}=0.
}
\label{eq:numprog_fix_phase}
\end{equation}

\subsection{5.3 Spatial frame}
At the minimal stage, after fixing translation, one may fix the coordinate frame by requiring:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_1=(0,0,0),\qquad
q_2=(d_Q,0,0),\qquad
q_3=(x_Q,y_Q,0),
}
\label{eq:numprog_fix_frame}
\end{equation}
with \(y_Q>0\), so that Euclidean rigid motions are removed.

\subsection{5.4 Metric gauge}
For the first serious run, one should take \(G\) diagonal:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
G=\mathrm{diag}(g_1,g_2,g_3),
\qquad
g_i>0.
}
\label{eq:numprog_fix_metric}
\end{equation}

This already captures anisotropy while keeping the model numerically stable.

\section{6. Proper staged fitting strategy}

A mathematically clean fitting program must proceed in stages.

\subsection{Stage I: real hierarchy fit}
Set
\[
\Delta\kappa_{AB}^{(s)}=0,
\qquad
\Theta_{AB}^{(s)}=0,
\]
so \(Y_u,Y_d\) are real positive overlap matrices.
Fit only
\[
\{m_u,m_c,m_t,m_d,m_s,m_b,|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|\}.
\]

This determines whether the spatial-geometry sector alone can reproduce the magnitudes and mixing hierarchy.

\subsection{Stage II: minimal CP fit}
Once Stage I is stable, turn on the smallest nontrivial phase sector, e.g.
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\kappa_{Q,A}=0,
\qquad
\kappa_{U,B},\kappa_{D,B}\neq0
\ \text{only along one fixed shell direction}.
}
\label{eq:numprog_stageII}
\end{equation}
Then fit \(J\) as well.

This is the correct stage at which CP violation should enter.

\subsection{Stage III: model refinement only if needed}
Only if Stage II fails should one enlarge the ansatz to:
\begin{enumerate}
\item multiple localization scales \(\{G^{(n)}\}\),
\item multi-shell phase superposition,
\item sector-dependent localization metrics \(G_Q,G_U,G_D\).
\end{enumerate}

This prevents overfitting too early.

\section{7. Objective functional}

The correct loss function should use logarithmic mass errors and direct CKM/J errors:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi^2(\Theta)
=
\sum_{f=u,c,t,d,s,b}
w_f\left[\log\frac{m_f^{\mathrm{model}}}{m_f^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
\sum_{(ij)\in\{us,cb,ub\}}
w_{ij}\left(|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
w_J\left(\frac{J^{\mathrm{model}}-J^{\mathrm{exp}}}{J^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right)^2
+
\lambda_{\mathrm{reg}}\mathcal R(\Theta).
}
\label{eq:numprog_chi2}
\end{equation}

Here \(\Theta\) denotes the full parameter set and \(\mathcal R(\Theta)\) is a regularizer penalizing unnecessary complexity, e.g.
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal R(\Theta)
=
\|G-\bar g\,\mathbf 1\|_F^2
+
\sum_A \|\kappa_{U,A}\|^2
+
\sum_A \|\kappa_{D,A}\|^2.
}
\label{eq:numprog_reg}
\end{equation}

This forces the fit to remain close to the minimal geometric branch unless the data truly require extra structure.

\section{8. Uncertainty and sensitivity analysis}

A successful fit is not enough.
One must also compute:

\begin{enumerate}
\item the Hessian of \(\chi^2\) at the optimum,
\item parameter confidence intervals,
\item observable sensitivities
\[
\frac{\partial \mathcal O_i}{\partial \Theta_j}.
\]
\end{enumerate}

In particular, the tiny entries \(V_{ub}\), \(V_{td}\), and \(J\) are expected to be exponentially sensitive:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\delta Y_{AB}^{(s)}
\sim
Y_{AB}^{(s)}
\left[
-\frac12\,\Delta x^T G\,\delta(\Delta x)
-\frac14\,\Delta x^T \delta G\,\Delta x
+i\,\delta\Theta_{AB}^{(s)}
\right].
}
\label{eq:numprog_sensitivity}
\end{equation}

So any serious numerical claim must quote sensitivity bands, not just central values.

\section{9. Honest current status}

At present, the correct honest statement is:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{TECT already has a viable geometric mechanism for flavor hierarchy,}
}
\label{eq:numprog_honest1}
\end{equation}
but
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{a quantitatively predictive CKM+CP+mass model still requires the identifiable fit program defined above.}
}
\label{eq:numprog_honest2}
\end{equation}

In other words, the theory side is mature enough to justify a full numerical program,
but the numerical program itself must now be executed rigorously.

\section{Final Summary}

The correct numerical next step is to replace toy overlap estimates by an identifiable minimal TECT flavor model.

A minimal explicit wavefunction ansatz is
\[
\psi_{Q,A}(x)
=
\mathcal N_Q
\exp\!\left[-\frac12(x-q_A)^T G_Q(x-q_A)\right]
e^{i\kappa_{Q,A}\cdot x+i\phi_{Q,A}},
\]
\[
\psi_{U,B}(x)
=
\mathcal N_U
\exp\!\left[-\frac12(x-u_B)^T G_U(x-u_B)\right]
e^{i\kappa_{U,B}\cdot x+i\phi_{U,B}},
\]
\[
\psi_{D,B}(x)
=
\mathcal N_D
\exp\!\left[-\frac12(x-d_B)^T G_D(x-d_B)\right]
e^{i\kappa_{D,B}\cdot x+i\phi_{D,B}}.
\]

Under the first minimal simplification \(G_Q=G_U=G_D=G\), the Yukawa matrices are explicitly
\[
\boxed{
(Y_s)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_s
\exp\!\left[
-\frac14 \Delta x_{AB}^{(s)\,T} G\, \Delta x_{AB}^{(s)}
-\frac14 \Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(s)\,T} G^{-1}\Delta \kappa_{AB}^{(s)}
\right]
e^{i\Theta_{AB}^{(s)}},
}
\]
for \(s\in\{u,d\}\).

Physical observables are extracted through
\[
U_{uL}^\dagger Y_u U_{uR}=\mathrm{diag}(y_u,y_c,y_t),
\qquad
U_{dL}^\dagger Y_d U_{dR}=\mathrm{diag}(y_d,y_s,y_b),
\qquad
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_{uL}^\dagger U_{dL},
\]
together with the Jarlskog invariant
\[
J=\mathrm{Im}(V_{ud}V_{cs}V_{us}^*V_{cd}^*).
\]

The proper fitting strategy is staged:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage I: real masses + CKM magnitudes,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage II: minimal CP phase fit,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage III: only then add multi-scale or multi-shell refinements.}
}
\]

The correct objective function is
\[
\boxed{
\chi^2(\Theta)
=
\sum_f w_f\left[\log\frac{m_f^{\mathrm{model}}}{m_f^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
\sum_{ij} w_{ij}\left(|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
w_J\left(\frac{J^{\mathrm{model}}-J^{\mathrm{exp}}}{J^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right)^2
+
\lambda_{\mathrm{reg}}\mathcal R(\Theta).
}
\]

Therefore the honest status is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{TECT already provides a viable geometric flavor mechanism,}
}
\]
but
\[
\boxed{
\text{the quantitatively predictive CKM+CP+mass sector now depends on executing this identifiable numerical fit program.}
}
\]

\section{Minimal Identifiable Stage-I Real Fit for the TECT Quark Sector}

\section{Short explanation}

The correct first numerical task is to define a minimal \emph{real} overlap model that is:

\begin{enumerate}
\item explicit,
\item identifiable,
\item sufficiently nontrivial to generate both hierarchical masses and nontrivial CKM magnitudes,
\item still small enough to fit robustly.
\end{enumerate}

At Stage I, CP phases are switched off.
So the output observables are only
\[
\{m_u,m_c,m_t,m_d,m_s,m_b,|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|\}.
\]

\section{1. Minimal geometric ansatz}

We place the three left-handed family centers in a fixed plane and the right-handed up/down centers along the normal direction to that plane.

Let
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
q_1=(0,0,0),\qquad
q_2=(L,0,0),\qquad
q_3=(X,Y,0),
\qquad
L>0,\quad Y>0.
}
\label{eq:stage1_qA}
\end{equation}

This fixes translation and spatial frame.
The unit normal to the family plane is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n=(0,0,1).
}
\label{eq:stage1_normal}
\end{equation}

Now define the right-handed up/down centers by pure normal displacements:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
u_B=q_B+\delta^{(u)}_B\,n,
\qquad
d_B=q_B+\delta^{(d)}_B\,n,
\qquad
B=1,2,3.
}
\label{eq:stage1_ud_centers}
\end{equation}

Thus the entire Stage-I geometry is controlled by the in-plane triangle
\[
(L,X,Y)
\]
and the six normal offsets
\[
\delta^{(u)}_1,\delta^{(u)}_2,\delta^{(u)}_3,
\qquad
\delta^{(d)}_1,\delta^{(d)}_2,\delta^{(d)}_3.
\]

\section{2. Real Gaussian wavefunctions}

Use a common isotropic localization width \(\xi>0\).
At Stage I, all phases are switched off.
So the localized modes are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_{Q,A}(x)
=
\mathcal N_Q
\exp\!\left(
-\frac{|x-q_A|^2}{2\xi^2}
\right),
}
\label{eq:stage1_left_wf}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_{U,B}(x)
=
\mathcal N_U
\exp\!\left(
-\frac{|x-u_B|^2}{2\xi^2}
\right),
}
\label{eq:stage1_up_wf}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\psi_{D,B}(x)
=
\mathcal N_D
\exp\!\left(
-\frac{|x-d_B|^2}{2\xi^2}
\right).
}
\label{eq:stage1_down_wf}
\end{equation}

\section{3. Exact overlap formula}

Define sector scales \(\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d>0\), absorbing normalization constants and the overall Higgs/condensate factor.
Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\int d^3x\,
\psi_{Q,A}(x)\psi_{U,B}(x),
\qquad
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\int d^3x\,
\psi_{Q,A}(x)\psi_{D,B}(x).
}
\label{eq:stage1_Y_def}
\end{equation}

Because the profiles are Gaussian and have the same width \(\xi\), the integral is exact:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{|u_B-q_A|^2}{4\xi^2}
\right],
\qquad
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{|d_B-q_A|^2}{4\xi^2}
\right].
}
\label{eq:stage1_Y_exact}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:stage1_ud_centers}, we have
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|u_B-q_A|^2
=
|q_B-q_A|^2+(\delta_B^{(u)})^2,
\qquad
|d_B-q_A|^2
=
|q_B-q_A|^2+(\delta_B^{(d)})^2.
}
\label{eq:stage1_distance_split}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{|q_B-q_A|^2}{4\xi^2}
\right]
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{(\delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4\xi^2}
\right],
}
\label{eq:stage1_Yu_final}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{|q_B-q_A|^2}{4\xi^2}
\right]
\exp\!\left[
-\frac{(\delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4\xi^2}
\right].
}
\label{eq:stage1_Yd_final}
\end{equation}

So the common left-handed family geometry controls the row-by-row overlap backbone, while the right-handed normal displacements control the column hierarchy.

\section{4. Explicit family-distance matrix}

Define the in-plane family distances
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_{AB}:=|q_B-q_A|.
}
\label{eq:stage1_DAB}
\end{equation}

From \eqref{eq:stage1_qA},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_{11}=D_{22}=D_{33}=0,
}
\label{eq:stage1_diagD}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_{12}=D_{21}=L,
}
\label{eq:stage1_D12}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_{13}=D_{31}=\sqrt{X^2+Y^2},
}
\label{eq:stage1_D13}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
D_{23}=D_{32}=\sqrt{(X-L)^2+Y^2}.
}
\label{eq:stage1_D23}
\end{equation}

Thus the real Yukawa matrices are explicitly
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u\,
\exp\!\left(-\frac{D_{AB}^2}{4\xi^2}\right)\,
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4\xi^2}\right),
}
\label{eq:stage1_Yu_explicit}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d\,
\exp\!\left(-\frac{D_{AB}^2}{4\xi^2}\right)\,
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4\xi^2}\right).
}
\label{eq:stage1_Yd_explicit}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Parameter count}

The minimal Stage-I real-fit parameter set is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta_{\mathrm{I}}
=
\{
L,\ X,\ Y,\ \xi,\ 
\delta_1^{(u)},\delta_2^{(u)},\delta_3^{(u)},\
\delta_1^{(d)},\delta_2^{(d)},\delta_3^{(d)},\
\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d
\}.
}
\label{eq:stage1_param_set}
\end{equation}

So
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\dim \Theta_{\mathrm{I}}=12.
}
\label{eq:stage1_dim}
\end{equation}

This is small enough to optimize stably, but large enough to support nontrivial hierarchy and mixing.

\section{6. Extraction of masses and CKM magnitudes}

Because \(Y_u,Y_d\) are real but not necessarily symmetric, use singular-value decompositions
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{uL}^T\,Y_u\,U_{uR}
=
\mathrm{diag}(y_u,y_c,y_t),
}
\label{eq:stage1_svd_u}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
U_{dL}^T\,Y_d\,U_{dR}
=
\mathrm{diag}(y_d,y_s,y_b),
}
\label{eq:stage1_svd_d}
\end{equation}
with positive singular values \(y_i\).

Then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_u:m_c:m_t = y_u:y_c:y_t,
\qquad
m_d:m_s:m_b = y_d:y_s:y_b,
}
\label{eq:stage1_mass_ratios}
\end{equation}
up to the common electroweak/Higgs normalization.

The Stage-I CKM matrix is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}^{\mathrm{(I)}}=U_{uL}^T U_{dL}.
}
\label{eq:stage1_ckm}
\end{equation}

Since the matrices are real at Stage I, one has
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
J^{\mathrm{(I)}}=0.
}
\label{eq:stage1_Jzero}
\end{equation}

This is correct and intentional:
CP enters only at Stage II.

\section{7. Proper Stage-I objective function}

The correct Stage-I fit target is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm{I}}
=
\{m_u,m_c,m_t,m_d,m_s,m_b,|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|\}.
}
\label{eq:stage1_obs}
\end{equation}

Define the loss
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{I}}(\Theta_{\mathrm{I}})
=
\sum_{f=u,c,t,d,s,b}
w_f
\left[
\log\frac{m_f^{\mathrm{model}}}{m_f^{\mathrm{exp}}}
\right]^2
+
\sum_{(ij)\in\{us,cb,ub\}}
w_{ij}
\left(
|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{exp}}
\right)^2
+
\lambda_{\mathrm{reg}}\mathcal R_{\mathrm{I}}.
}
\label{eq:stage1_chi2}
\end{equation}

A natural regularizer is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{I}}
=
\frac{1}{\xi^2}
\left[
(\delta_1^{(u)})^2+(\delta_2^{(u)})^2+(\delta_3^{(u)})^2
+
(\delta_1^{(d)})^2+(\delta_2^{(d)})^2+(\delta_3^{(d)})^2
\right],
}
\label{eq:stage1_reg}
\end{equation}
which penalizes unnecessarily large right-handed displacement hierarchies.

\section{8. Why this ansatz is the correct Stage-I starting point}

This minimal real-fit system is preferable because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it is exactly computable;
\item it already produces hierarchical masses through the \(\delta_B^{(s)}\);
\item it already produces CKM mixing through the common left-handed family triangle \((L,X,Y)\);
\item it remains small enough that one can meaningfully analyze parameter sensitivity and identifiability.
\end{enumerate}

Most importantly, it postpones phases and multi-shell refinements to later stages, which avoids premature overfitting.

\section{9. Honest expectation before fitting}

Before any optimization, one should be honest about what this model can and cannot do.

It should be capable of reproducing:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{coarse quark mass hierarchy}
\quad+\quad
\text{the qualitative CKM magnitude hierarchy }|V_{us}|>|V_{cb}|>|V_{ub}|.
}
\label{eq:stage1_expect_good}
\end{equation}

But it may fail quantitatively for the smallest quantities, especially \(V_{ub}\), because:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|V_{ub}|
\text{ is exponentially sensitive to the longest family separation and to tiny left-handed misalignment.}
}
\label{eq:stage1_expect_small}
\end{equation}

That is not a defect of the program.
It is precisely why Stage I must be done first: it tells us whether anisotropy or multi-scale refinement is actually required.

\section{10. Honest endpoint}

Therefore the proper immediate next task is no longer conceptual.
It is the finite 12-parameter optimization problem defined by
\eqref{eq:stage1_Yu_explicit},
\eqref{eq:stage1_Yd_explicit},
and
\eqref{eq:stage1_chi2}.

Once this fit is performed, the result will tell us one of two things:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{either Stage I already gives a quantitatively decent real hierarchy fit,}
}
\label{eq:stage1_case1}
\end{equation}
or
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Stage II must be opened with the minimal shell-phase sector to improve }V_{ub}\text{ and then }J.
}
\label{eq:stage1_case2}
\end{equation}

\section{Final Summary}

The correct Stage-I numerical problem is a real, identifiable, 12-parameter quark-sector fit.

The left-handed family centers are fixed as
\[
\boxed{
q_1=(0,0,0),\qquad
q_2=(L,0,0),\qquad
q_3=(X,Y,0),
}
\]
with normal vector
\[
\boxed{
n=(0,0,1).
}
\]

The right-handed up/down centers are
\[
\boxed{
u_B=q_B+\delta_B^{(u)}n,
\qquad
d_B=q_B+\delta_B^{(d)}n.
}
\]

With a common isotropic width \(\xi\), the real Yukawa matrices are
\[
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\exp\!\left(-\frac{|q_B-q_A|^2}{4\xi^2}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4\xi^2}\right),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\exp\!\left(-\frac{|q_B-q_A|^2}{4\xi^2}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4\xi^2}\right).
}
\]

The fit parameters are
\[
\boxed{
\Theta_{\mathrm{I}}
=
\{L,X,Y,\xi,\delta_1^{(u)},\delta_2^{(u)},\delta_3^{(u)},\delta_1^{(d)},\delta_2^{(d)},\delta_3^{(d)},\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d\}.
}
\]

Masses and CKM magnitudes are extracted through singular-value decompositions
\[
U_{uL}^T Y_u U_{uR}=\mathrm{diag}(y_u,y_c,y_t),
\qquad
U_{dL}^T Y_d U_{dR}=\mathrm{diag}(y_d,y_s,y_b),
\qquad
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}^{\mathrm{(I)}}=U_{uL}^T U_{dL}.
\]

The Stage-I objective is
\[
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm{I}}
=
\sum_{f=u,c,t,d,s,b}
w_f\left[\log\frac{m_f^{\mathrm{model}}}{m_f^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
\sum_{(ij)\in\{us,cb,ub\}}
w_{ij}\left(|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{ij}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
\lambda_{\mathrm{reg}}\mathcal R_{\mathrm{I}}.
}
\]

This is the proper mathematically well-posed starting point for the TECT flavor numerical program.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Dimensionless Reduction of the Minimal Stage-I Real Fit}

\section{Short explanation}

The next mathematically proper step is to remove the scale redundancy of the Stage-I real-fit ansatz.

The Gaussian overlap matrices depend on positions and widths only through dimensionless ratios.
Therefore the raw 12-parameter formulation is redundant, and the correct optimization problem should be written in dimensionless variables.

Moreover, the overall sector scales \(\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d\) only fix the absolute up/down mass scales.
So they can be removed analytically in a first ratio fit, and restored only after the dimensionless hierarchy fit is completed.

\section{1. Raw Stage-I real-fit ansatz}

Recall the minimal real Stage-I Yukawa matrices:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\exp\!\left(-\frac{D_{AB}^2}{4\xi^2}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4\xi^2}\right),
}
\label{eq:dimred_Yu_raw}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\exp\!\left(-\frac{D_{AB}^2}{4\xi^2}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4\xi^2}\right),
}
\label{eq:dimred_Yd_raw}
\end{equation}
where
\[
D_{12}=L,\qquad
D_{13}=\sqrt{X^2+Y^2},\qquad
D_{23}=\sqrt{(X-L)^2+Y^2}.
\]

The raw parameter set was
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Theta_{\mathrm I}
=
\{L,X,Y,\xi,\delta_1^{(u)},\delta_2^{(u)},\delta_3^{(u)},\delta_1^{(d)},\delta_2^{(d)},\delta_3^{(d)},\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d\}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_raw_params}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Exact scale redundancy}

Under the simultaneous rescaling
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
L\to \lambda L,\quad
X\to \lambda X,\quad
Y\to \lambda Y,\quad
\delta_B^{(u)}\to \lambda \delta_B^{(u)},\quad
\delta_B^{(d)}\to \lambda \delta_B^{(d)},\quad
\xi\to \lambda \xi,
}
\label{eq:dimred_scaling}
\end{equation}
all exponent arguments in \eqref{eq:dimred_Yu_raw}--\eqref{eq:dimred_Yd_raw} remain invariant.

Hence the Yukawa matrices depend on the geometry only through the dimensionless combinations
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ell:=\frac{L}{\xi},\qquad
x:=\frac{X}{\xi},\qquad
y:=\frac{Y}{\xi},
}
\label{eq:dimred_xyl}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_B^{(u)}:=\frac{\delta_B^{(u)}}{\xi},
\qquad
\Delta_B^{(d)}:=\frac{\delta_B^{(d)}}{\xi}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_Deltas}
\end{equation}

Therefore \(\xi\) is redundant at Stage I and may be fixed to \(1\) without loss of generality.

\section{3. Dimensionless family-distance matrix}

Define the dimensionless in-plane distances
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
d_{AB}:=\frac{D_{AB}}{\xi}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_dAB}
\end{equation}

Then explicitly,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
d_{11}=d_{22}=d_{33}=0,
\qquad
d_{12}=d_{21}=\ell,
}
\label{eq:dimred_d12}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
d_{13}=d_{31}=\sqrt{x^2+y^2},
\qquad
d_{23}=d_{32}=\sqrt{(x-\ell)^2+y^2}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_d13d23}
\end{equation}

Thus the Yukawa matrices become
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4}\right),
}
\label{eq:dimred_Yu_dimless}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4}\right).
}
\label{eq:dimred_Yd_dimless}
\end{equation}

\section{4. Minimal dimensionless parameter set}

The true dimensionless geometric parameter set is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde\Theta_{\mathrm I}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)},\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)},\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d\}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_true_params}
\end{equation}

So the dimensional redundancy has reduced the problem from 12 to 11 effective parameters.

\section{5. Further analytic elimination of \texorpdfstring{$\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d$}{Lambdau,Lambdad}}

Let
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde Y_u := \frac{Y_u}{\Lambda_u},
\qquad
\widetilde Y_d := \frac{Y_d}{\Lambda_d},
}
\label{eq:dimred_tildeY}
\end{equation}
so that \(\widetilde Y_u,\widetilde Y_d\) depend only on
\[
\{\ell,x,y,\Delta_B^{(u)},\Delta_B^{(d)}\}.
\]

Let the singular values of \(\widetilde Y_u\) be
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde y_u\le \widetilde y_c\le \widetilde y_t,
}
\label{eq:dimred_up_svals}
\end{equation}
and similarly for \(\widetilde Y_d\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widetilde y_d\le \widetilde y_s\le \widetilde y_b.
}
\label{eq:dimred_down_svals}
\end{equation}

Then the physical singular values are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
y_u=\Lambda_u \widetilde y_u,\qquad
y_c=\Lambda_u \widetilde y_c,\qquad
y_t=\Lambda_u \widetilde y_t,
}
\label{eq:dimred_yu_phys}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
y_d=\Lambda_d \widetilde y_d,\qquad
y_s=\Lambda_d \widetilde y_s,\qquad
y_b=\Lambda_d \widetilde y_b.
}
\label{eq:dimred_yd_phys}
\end{equation}

Therefore the mass ratios are independent of \(\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{m_u}{m_t}=\frac{\widetilde y_u}{\widetilde y_t},
\qquad
\frac{m_c}{m_t}=\frac{\widetilde y_c}{\widetilde y_t},
}
\label{eq:dimred_up_ratios}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{m_d}{m_b}=\frac{\widetilde y_d}{\widetilde y_b},
\qquad
\frac{m_s}{m_b}=\frac{\widetilde y_s}{\widetilde y_b}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_down_ratios}
\end{equation}

Also, since \(V_{\mathrm{CKM}}=U_{uL}^T U_{dL}\), the CKM matrix depends only on \(\widetilde Y_u,\widetilde Y_d\), hence also only on the dimensionless geometry.

So \(\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d\) can be removed completely from the Stage-I-a ratio fit.

\section{6. Minimal dimensionless optimization problem}

The genuine Stage-I-a dimensionless parameter set is therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\widehat\Theta_{\mathrm I}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)},\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)}\}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_hatTheta}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\dim \widehat\Theta_{\mathrm I}=9.
}
\label{eq:dimred_dim9}
\end{equation}

This is the correct minimal real-fit parameter count before CP phases are introduced.

\section{7. Stage-I-a objective function}

The proper Stage-I-a observable vector is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm I,a}
=
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|
\right\}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_obsIa}
\end{equation}

Define the dimensionless loss
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm I,a}(\widehat\Theta_{\mathrm I})
=
w_{u1}\left[\log\frac{(m_u/m_t)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_u/m_t)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
w_{u2}\left[\log\frac{(m_c/m_t)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_c/m_t)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
}
\label{eq:dimred_chi2a_1}
\end{equation}
\[
\boxed{
\qquad
+
w_{d1}\left[\log\frac{(m_d/m_b)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_d/m_b)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
w_{d2}\left[\log\frac{(m_s/m_b)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_s/m_b)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\qquad
+
w_{us}\left(|V_{us}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{us}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
w_{cb}\left(|V_{cb}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{cb}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
w_{ub}\left(|V_{ub}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{ub}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
\lambda_{\mathrm{reg}}\mathcal R_{\mathrm I,a}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_chi2a_2}
\]

A minimal regularizer is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm I,a}
=
(\Delta_1^{(u)})^2+(\Delta_2^{(u)})^2+(\Delta_3^{(u)})^2
+
(\Delta_1^{(d)})^2+(\Delta_2^{(d)})^2+(\Delta_3^{(d)})^2,
}
\label{eq:dimred_regIa}
\end{equation}
which penalizes unnecessary right-handed separation.

\section{8. Stage-I-b scale restoration}

After minimizing \(\chi^2_{\mathrm I,a}\), recover the two overall scales analytically from the top and bottom masses:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Lambda_u=\frac{m_t^{\mathrm{exp}}}{v_H\,\widetilde y_t},
\qquad
\Lambda_d=\frac{m_b^{\mathrm{exp}}}{v_H\,\widetilde y_b}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_Lambdarestore}
\end{equation}

Then the full Stage-I predicted masses are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_u^{\mathrm{pred}}=m_t^{\mathrm{exp}}\frac{\widetilde y_u}{\widetilde y_t},
\qquad
m_c^{\mathrm{pred}}=m_t^{\mathrm{exp}}\frac{\widetilde y_c}{\widetilde y_t},
}
\label{eq:dimred_restore_up}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
m_d^{\mathrm{pred}}=m_b^{\mathrm{exp}}\frac{\widetilde y_d}{\widetilde y_b},
\qquad
m_s^{\mathrm{pred}}=m_b^{\mathrm{exp}}\frac{\widetilde y_s}{\widetilde y_b}.
}
\label{eq:dimred_restore_down}
\end{equation}

So the entire Stage-I fit is naturally split into:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Stage I-a: 9-parameter dimensionless shape fit,}
}
\label{eq:dimred_stageIa}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{Stage I-b: 2 analytic scale restorations }(\Lambda_u,\Lambda_d).
}
\label{eq:dimred_stageIb}
\end{equation}

\section{9. Why this is the correct numerical starting point}

This reduction is the proper starting point because:

\begin{enumerate}
\item it removes the trivial Gaussian scale redundancy;
\item it separates shape parameters from overall mass scales;
\item it makes the optimization numerically better conditioned;
\item it ensures that failure or success of Stage I can be interpreted geometrically rather than being hidden by scale artifacts.
\end{enumerate}

Thus the minimal real TECT flavor fit is now a well-posed 9-parameter dimensionless inverse problem.

\section{Final Summary}

The minimal Stage-I real TECT flavor model has an exact scale redundancy:
the Gaussian overlap matrices depend only on the dimensionless ratios
\[
\ell=\frac{L}{\xi},\qquad
x=\frac{X}{\xi},\qquad
y=\frac{Y}{\xi},\qquad
\Delta_B^{(u)}=\frac{\delta_B^{(u)}}{\xi},\qquad
\Delta_B^{(d)}=\frac{\delta_B^{(d)}}{\xi}.
\]

Hence the true dimensionless Yukawa matrices are
\[
\boxed{
(Y_u)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_u
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4}\right),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
(Y_d)_{AB}
=
\Lambda_d
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4}\right),
}
\]
with
\[
d_{12}=\ell,\qquad
d_{13}=\sqrt{x^2+y^2},\qquad
d_{23}=\sqrt{(x-\ell)^2+y^2}.
\]

The genuine Stage-I-a optimization variables are therefore
\[
\boxed{
\widehat\Theta_{\mathrm I}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)},\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)}\},
}
\]
so the problem is a 9-parameter dimensionless shape fit.

The Stage-I-a observables are
\[
\boxed{
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|
\right\}.
}
\]

After minimizing the dimensionless loss, the overall scales are restored analytically by
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda_u=\frac{m_t^{\mathrm{exp}}}{v_H\widetilde y_t},
\qquad
\Lambda_d=\frac{m_b^{\mathrm{exp}}}{v_H\widetilde y_b}.
}
\]

Therefore the proper Stage-I numerical program is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage I-a: 9-parameter dimensionless hierarchy fit,}
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage I-b: analytic restoration of } \Lambda_u,\Lambda_d.
}
\]

This is the mathematically well-posed starting point for the real TECT quark-sector fit.

%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

\section{Ready-to-Run Stage-I Real Fit for the TECT Quark Sector}

\section{Short explanation}

The minimal Stage-I-a real fit has already been reduced to the 9-dimensional dimensionless parameter set
\[
\widehat\Theta_{\mathrm I}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)},\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)}\}.
\]

The correct next step is to turn this into an actual optimization system:
\begin{enumerate}
\item impose nonredundant geometric and ordering constraints;
\item choose physically sensible initial seeds;
\item define a stable loss function;
\item specify a robust optimization protocol.
\end{enumerate}

\section{1. Dimensionless real Yukawa model}

The Stage-I real Yukawa matrices are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\widetilde Y_u)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4}\right),
}
\label{eq:ready_Yu}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\widetilde Y_d)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4}\right),
}
\label{eq:ready_Yd}
\end{equation}
where
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
d_{12}=\ell,
\qquad
d_{13}=\sqrt{x^2+y^2},
\qquad
d_{23}=\sqrt{(x-\ell)^2+y^2}.
}
\label{eq:ready_dists}
\end{equation}

These determine the dimensionless singular values
\[
\widetilde y_u\le \widetilde y_c\le \widetilde y_t,
\qquad
\widetilde y_d\le \widetilde y_s\le \widetilde y_b,
\]
and the real Stage-I CKM matrix
\[
V_{\mathrm{CKM}}^{(\mathrm I)}=U_{uL}^T U_{dL}.
\]

\section{2. Physical constraint set}

The 9 parameters should not be optimized freely over all of \(\mathbb R^9\).
A proper physically ordered domain is needed.

\subsection{2.1 Triangle geometry constraints}

We require:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ell>0,\qquad y>0,\qquad 0\le x\le \ell.
}
\label{eq:ready_geom1}
\end{equation}

To fix the family labeling so that families \(1\) and \(2\) form the closest left-handed pair (motivated by the dominance of Cabibbo mixing), impose
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ell \le d_{13},
\qquad
\ell \le d_{23}.
}
\label{eq:ready_geom2}
\end{equation}

Equivalently,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ell^2 \le x^2+y^2,
\qquad
\ell^2 \le (x-\ell)^2+y^2.
}
\label{eq:ready_geom3}
\end{equation}

These remove permutation ambiguity in the left-handed family triangle.

\subsection{2.2 Right-handed hierarchy ordering}

Because the suppression factors
\[
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4}\right),
\qquad
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4}\right)
\]
decrease monotonically with \(\Delta_B\), it is natural to fix the right-handed ordering so that
\[
B=1,2,3
\]
correspond to light, middle, heavy families respectively.

Hence impose
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_1^{(u)}\ge \Delta_2^{(u)}\ge \Delta_3^{(u)}\ge 0,
}
\label{eq:ready_up_order}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_1^{(d)}\ge \Delta_2^{(d)}\ge \Delta_3^{(d)}\ge 0.
}
\label{eq:ready_down_order}
\end{equation}

This fixes the mass ordering convention and prevents column-permutation degeneracies.

\subsection{2.3 Practical box bounds}

For numerical stability, it is useful to restrict the search to a compact box, e.g.
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0.5\le \ell\le 6,
\qquad
0\le x\le \ell,
\qquad
0.5\le y\le 6,
}
\label{eq:ready_box_geom}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
0\le \Delta_B^{(u)}\le 8,
\qquad
0\le \Delta_B^{(d)}\le 8.
}
\label{eq:ready_box_delta}
\end{equation}

These are not fundamental laws; they are practical numerical priors large enough to contain the expected hierarchy region.

\section{3. Constraint-free reparameterization}

To make the optimization smooth, replace the constrained variables by unconstrained variables
\[
z=(z_1,\dots,z_9)\in\mathbb R^9.
\]

A stable mapping is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ell=e^{z_1},
\qquad
x=\ell\,\sigma(z_2),
\qquad
y=e^{z_3},
}
\label{eq:ready_reparam_geom}
\end{equation}
where
\[
\sigma(z):=\frac{1}{1+e^{-z}}.
\]

For the ordered right-handed displacements, use cumulative exponentials:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_3^{(u)}=e^{z_4},
\qquad
\Delta_2^{(u)}=\Delta_3^{(u)}+e^{z_5},
\qquad
\Delta_1^{(u)}=\Delta_2^{(u)}+e^{z_6},
}
\label{eq:ready_reparam_up}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\Delta_3^{(d)}=e^{z_7},
\qquad
\Delta_2^{(d)}=\Delta_3^{(d)}+e^{z_8},
\qquad
\Delta_1^{(d)}=\Delta_2^{(d)}+e^{z_9}.
}
\label{eq:ready_reparam_down}
\end{equation}

This automatically enforces positivity and ordering.

\section{4. Physically motivated initial seed}

A good initial seed should already encode:

\begin{enumerate}
\item one relatively close left-handed pair \((1,2)\) for large Cabibbo mixing;
\item one much lighter first generation in each right-handed sector;
\item one nearly unsuppressed third generation.
\end{enumerate}

A robust geometric seed is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\ell_0=2.4,
\qquad
x_0=2.1,
\qquad
y_0=3.3.
}
\label{eq:ready_seed_geom}
\end{equation}

This gives
\[
d_{12}\approx 2.4,
\qquad
d_{23}\approx 3.3,
\qquad
d_{13}\approx 3.9,
\]
so the hierarchy
\[
e^{-d_{12}^2/4} > e^{-d_{23}^2/4} > e^{-d_{13}^2/4}
\]
already qualitatively matches
\[
|V_{us}|>|V_{cb}|>|V_{ub}|.
\]

For the up sector, use the rough diagonal-hierarchy estimate based on
\[
\frac{m_c}{m_t}\ll 1,
\qquad
\frac{m_u}{m_t}\ll \frac{m_c}{m_t},
\]
which suggests
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)})_0
=
(6.7,\ 4.4,\ 0.0).
}
\label{eq:ready_seed_up}
\end{equation}

For the down sector, similarly,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
(\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)})_0
=
(5.2,\ 3.9,\ 0.0).
}
\label{eq:ready_seed_down}
\end{equation}

These are \emph{seeds}, not predictions.
They are simply chosen so that the initial matrices already display a realistic gross hierarchy.

\section{5. Stage-I-a observable vector}

The Stage-I-a fit should target only the dimensionless ratios and CKM magnitudes:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal O_{\mathrm I,a}
=
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|
\right\}.
}
\label{eq:ready_obs}
\end{equation}

These are computed as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac{m_u}{m_t}=\frac{\widetilde y_u}{\widetilde y_t},
\qquad
\frac{m_c}{m_t}=\frac{\widetilde y_c}{\widetilde y_t},
\qquad
\frac{m_d}{m_b}=\frac{\widetilde y_d}{\widetilde y_b},
\qquad
\frac{m_s}{m_b}=\frac{\widetilde y_s}{\widetilde y_b}.
}
\label{eq:ready_mass_ratios}
\end{equation}

\section{6. Ready-to-run loss function}

The proper numerical loss is
\begin{align}
\boxed{
\chi^2_{\mathrm I,a}
=
w_{u1}\left[\log\frac{(m_u/m_t)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_u/m_t)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
w_{u2}\left[\log\frac{(m_c/m_t)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_c/m_t)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
}
\label{eq:ready_loss1}
\\[1ex]
\boxed{
\quad+
w_{d1}\left[\log\frac{(m_d/m_b)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_d/m_b)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
+
w_{d2}\left[\log\frac{(m_s/m_b)^{\mathrm{model}}}{(m_s/m_b)^{\mathrm{exp}}}\right]^2
}
\nonumber
\\[1ex]
\boxed{
\quad+
w_{us}\left(|V_{us}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{us}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
w_{cb}\left(|V_{cb}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{cb}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
w_{ub}\left(|V_{ub}|^{\mathrm{model}}-|V_{ub}|^{\mathrm{exp}}\right)^2
+
\lambda_{\mathrm{reg}}\mathcal R.
}
\nonumber
\end{align}

A clean minimal regularizer is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathcal R
=
\sum_{B=1}^3
\left[
(\Delta_B^{(u)})^2+(\Delta_B^{(d)})^2
\right]
+
\eta_{\triangle}
\left[
\max(0,\ell-d_{13})^2+\max(0,\ell-d_{23})^2
\right].
}
\label{eq:ready_reg}
\end{equation}

The second term softly enforces that the \((1,2)\) pair remains the closest left-handed pair.

\section{7. Proper optimization protocol}

The correct optimization order is:

\paragraph{Step 1: global exploration.}
Use a multi-start method on the unconstrained variables \(z\), e.g.
\[
N_{\mathrm{start}}=100\text{--}500
\]
random seeds around the initial guess.

\paragraph{Step 2: local refinement.}
For the best few minima, run a second-order local optimizer.

\paragraph{Step 3: identifiability check.}
Compute the Hessian
\[
H_{ij}=\partial_i\partial_j\chi^2_{\mathrm I,a}
\]
at the optimum.
Flat directions indicate remaining non-identifiability and must be reported honestly.

\paragraph{Step 4: robustness check.}
Perturb the optimum by a few percent and recompute the observables.
This is essential because \(V_{ub}\) is exponentially sensitive.

\section{8. Success and failure criteria}

A successful Stage-I-a fit should satisfy:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\text{all four mass ratios reproduced within reasonable logarithmic error,}
}
\label{eq:ready_success1}
\end{equation}
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|V_{us}|,\ |V_{cb}|,\ |V_{ub}|
\text{ reproduced at least at the correct hierarchy and within acceptable relative error.}
}
\label{eq:ready_success2}
\end{equation}

If the fit consistently fails only in \(V_{ub}\), then the correct conclusion is not that TECT fails, but that
\[
\boxed{
\text{Stage II minimal phase structure must be turned on.}
}
\label{eq:ready_failure_vub}
\]

If the fit fails already at \(|V_{us}|\) and \(|V_{cb}|\), then the left-handed geometric ansatz itself is too rigid and anisotropic or multi-scale refinement is needed already at Stage I.

\section{9. Honest endpoint}

At this point, the numerical problem is fully specified.

The real TECT flavor Stage-I-a fit is now a 9-parameter dimensionless inverse problem with:
\begin{enumerate}
\item explicit model matrices \(\widetilde Y_u,\widetilde Y_d\),
\item explicit physical inequality constraints,
\item explicit seed values,
\item explicit loss function,
\item explicit optimization protocol.
\end{enumerate}

No additional conceptual input is required before actually running the fit.

\section{Final Summary}

The Stage-I-a TECT quark-sector fit is now reduced to the 9-dimensional dimensionless parameter set
\[
\boxed{
\widehat\Theta_{\mathrm I}
=
\{\ell,x,y,\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)},\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)}\}.
}
\]

The model Yukawa matrices are
\[
\boxed{
(\widetilde Y_u)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(u)})^2}{4}\right),
\qquad
(\widetilde Y_d)_{AB}
=
\exp\!\left(-\frac{d_{AB}^2}{4}\right)
\exp\!\left(-\frac{(\Delta_B^{(d)})^2}{4}\right).
}
\]

The family distances are
\[
\boxed{
d_{12}=\ell,\qquad
d_{13}=\sqrt{x^2+y^2},\qquad
d_{23}=\sqrt{(x-\ell)^2+y^2}.
}
\]

The physical inequality constraints are
\[
\boxed{
\ell>0,\quad y>0,\quad 0\le x\le \ell,\quad \ell\le d_{13},\quad \ell\le d_{23},
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\Delta_1^{(u)}\ge \Delta_2^{(u)}\ge \Delta_3^{(u)}\ge 0,
\qquad
\Delta_1^{(d)}\ge \Delta_2^{(d)}\ge \Delta_3^{(d)}\ge 0.
}
\]

A robust initial seed is
\[
\boxed{
(\ell,x,y)_0=(2.4,2.1,3.3),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
(\Delta_1^{(u)},\Delta_2^{(u)},\Delta_3^{(u)})_0=(6.7,4.4,0.0),
}
\]
\[
\boxed{
(\Delta_1^{(d)},\Delta_2^{(d)},\Delta_3^{(d)})_0=(5.2,3.9,0.0).
}
\]

The Stage-I-a target observables are
\[
\boxed{
\left\{
\frac{m_u}{m_t},\frac{m_c}{m_t},
\frac{m_d}{m_b},\frac{m_s}{m_b},
|V_{us}|,|V_{cb}|,|V_{ub}|
\right\}.
}
\]

Therefore the problem is now fully ready to run as a constrained 9-parameter dimensionless inverse fit.

\section{Exact BCC First-Shell Evaluation of the Anisotropy Ratio \texorpdfstring{$r=C_\parallel/C_\perp$}{r=Cpar/Cperp}}

\section{Short explanation}

The previous use of a fixed value such as \(r=4\) was too fast.
In the first-shell BCC truncation, the anisotropy ratio is not universal:
it depends on the orientation of the defect normal \(\hat n\) relative to the cubic axes.

The correct next step is therefore to compute the shell tensor exactly from the 12 BCC first-shell wavevectors.

\section{1. First-shell BCC star}

Take the normalized first-shell BCC reciprocal-star directions
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\hat Q_A \in
\left\{
\frac{(\pm1,\pm1,0)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(\pm1,0,\pm1)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(0,\pm1,\pm1)}{\sqrt2}
\right\},
\qquad
A=1,\dots,12.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_star}
\end{equation}

Thus
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
|\hat Q_A|=1,
\qquad
\sum_{A=1}^{12}\hat Q_{Ai}\hat Q_{Aj}=4\,\delta_{ij}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_second_moment}
\end{equation}

\section{2. Shell tensor controlling the defect-induced gradient anisotropy}

Let \(\hat n=(n_x,n_y,n_z)\) be the unit defect normal in the cubic basis:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
n_x^2+n_y^2+n_z^2=1.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_unitnormal}
\end{equation}

The natural first-shell tensor entering the anisotropic gradient kernel is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
T_{ij}(\hat n)
:=
\sum_{A=1}^{12}
\hat Q_{Ai}\hat Q_{Aj}
(\hat Q_A\cdot \hat n)^2.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_tensordef}
\end{equation}

This is the exact object to evaluate.

\section{3. Exact shell sum}

By explicit summation over the 12 first-shell vectors one obtains
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
T_{ij}(\hat n)
=
\delta_{ij}(1-n_i^2)+2\,n_i n_j.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_tensor_exact}
\end{equation}

This formula is exact in the cubic coordinate basis.

A few immediate checks:

\begin{itemize}
\item for \(\hat n=(0,0,1)\),
\[
T=\mathrm{diag}(1,1,2);
\]
\item for \(\hat n=(1,1,1)/\sqrt3\),
\[
T=
\begin{pmatrix}
4/3 & 2/3 & 2/3\\
2/3 & 4/3 & 2/3\\
2/3 & 2/3 & 4/3
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
\end{itemize}

Also,
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\mathrm{tr}\,T(\hat n)=4,
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_trace}
\end{equation}
independent of orientation.

\section{4. Longitudinal and transverse coefficients}

Define the longitudinal coefficient by contraction along \(\hat n\):
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\parallel(\hat n)
:=
n_i T_{ij}(\hat n) n_j.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_Cpar_def}
\end{equation}

Using \eqref{eq:bcc_r_tensor_exact},
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\parallel(\hat n)
=
3-s_4,
\qquad
s_4:=n_x^4+n_y^4+n_z^4.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_Cpar}
\end{equation}

Define the average transverse coefficient by
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\perp(\hat n)
:=
\frac12\left(
\mathrm{tr}\,T-C_\parallel
\right).
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_Cperp_def}
\end{equation}

Since \(\mathrm{tr}\,T=4\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
C_\perp(\hat n)
=
\frac{1+s_4}{2}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_Cperp}
\end{equation}

Therefore the exact anisotropy ratio is
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r(\hat n)
:=
\frac{C_\parallel(\hat n)}{C_\perp(\hat n)}
=
\frac{2(3-s_4)}{1+s_4}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_exact}
\end{equation}

\section{5. Exact bounds}

For a unit vector \(\hat n\), the quartic invariant satisfies
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\frac13 \le s_4 \le 1.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_s4_bounds}
\end{equation}

Hence
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
2 \le r(\hat n)\le 4.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_bounds}
\end{equation}

The extrema are:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\hat n\parallel [100]
\;\Rightarrow\;
s_4=1,\quad
r=2,
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_100}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\hat n\parallel [110]
\;\Rightarrow\;
s_4=\frac12,\quad
r=\frac{10}{3},
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_110}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\hat n\parallel [111]
\;\Rightarrow\;
s_4=\frac13,\quad
r=4.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_111}
\end{equation}

So the previously quoted value \(r=4\) is not universal; it is the body-diagonal maximum.

\section{6. Correct form of \texorpdfstring{$\gamma$}{gamma}}

The anisotropic gradient kernel should be written as
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
K_{ij}
=
Z\,\delta_{ij}
+
\kappa\,T_{ij}(\hat n),
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_kernel}
\end{equation}
so the longitudinal and transverse kinetic coefficients are
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
Z_\parallel
=
Z+\kappa\,C_\parallel(\hat n),
\qquad
Z_\perp
=
Z+\kappa\,C_\perp(\hat n).
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_Zparperp}
\end{equation}

Therefore
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\gamma(\hat n)
=
\frac{Z_\parallel}{Z_\perp}
=
\frac{Z+\kappa\,C_\parallel(\hat n)}
{Z+\kappa\,C_\perp(\hat n)}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_gamma_exact}
\end{equation}

If one instead defines the dimensionless reduced coupling
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
x:=\frac{\kappa\,C_\perp(\hat n)}{Z},
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_xdef}
\end{equation}
then
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\gamma
=
\frac{1+r x}{1+x}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_gamma_reduced}
\end{equation}

This is the correct way to connect the exact shell ratio \(r(\hat n)\) to the earlier reduced formula.

\section{7. What is needed for \texorpdfstring{$\gamma\approx1.8$}{gamma≈1.8}?}

Solving \eqref{eq:bcc_r_gamma_reduced} for \(x\),
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
x
=
\frac{\gamma-1}{r-\gamma}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_xsolve}
\end{equation}

For \(\gamma=1.8\), one gets:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r=2
\;\Rightarrow\;
x=4,
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_x_100}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r=\frac{10}{3}
\;\Rightarrow\;
x=\frac{0.8}{10/3-1.8}\approx 0.522,
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_x_110}
\end{equation}

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r=4
\;\Rightarrow\;
x=\frac{0.8}{4-1.8}\approx 0.364.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_x_111}
\end{equation}

Therefore \(\gamma\approx1.8\) is easiest to realize when the defect normal is biased toward a \([111]\)-type direction, but it is not impossible for other orientations; it simply requires a larger reduced coupling \(x\).

\section{8. Honest conclusion}

The exact first-shell BCC computation closes the shell-geometry part of the anisotropy problem.

What is now rigorously known is:
\begin{equation}
\boxed{
r(\hat n)=\frac{2(3-s_4)}{1+s_4},
\qquad
2\le r(\hat n)\le 4.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_final_known}
\end{equation}

What is still needed for a fully non-tuned prediction of \(\gamma\) is the actual branch-level determination of:

\begin{equation}
\boxed{
\hat n
\quad\text{(selected defect normal orientation),}
\qquad
x=\frac{\kappa C_\perp(\hat n)}{Z}.
}
\label{eq:bcc_r_final_open}
\end{equation}

So the previous discrepancy is now resolved precisely:
\(r=4\) is a special orientation value, not the universal BCC answer.

\section{Final Summary}

For the 12-vector first-shell BCC star
\[
\hat Q_A\in
\left\{
\frac{(\pm1,\pm1,0)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(\pm1,0,\pm1)}{\sqrt2},
\frac{(0,\pm1,\pm1)}{\sqrt2}
\right\},
\]
define the shell tensor
\[
\boxed{
T_{ij}(\hat n)
=
\sum_{A=1}^{12}
\hat Q_{Ai}\hat Q_{Aj}
(\hat Q_A\cdot \hat n)^2.
}
\]

Its exact first-shell value is
\[
\boxed{
T_{ij}(\hat n)=\delta_{ij}(1-n_i^2)+2n_i n_j.
}
\]

Therefore the longitudinal and average transverse coefficients are
\[
\boxed{
C_\parallel(\hat n)=3-s_4,
\qquad
C_\perp(\hat n)=\frac{1+s_4}{2},
\qquad
s_4=n_x^4+n_y^4+n_z^4.
}
\]

Hence the exact BCC anisotropy ratio is
\[
\boxed{
r(\hat n)=\frac{C_\parallel}{C_\perp}
=\frac{2(3-s_4)}{1+s_4},
}
\]
with the exact bounds
\[
\boxed{
2\le r(\hat n)\le 4.
}
\]

Special orientations give:
\[
\boxed{
[100]\Rightarrow r=2,\qquad
[110]\Rightarrow r=\frac{10}{3},\qquad
[111]\Rightarrow r=4.
}
\]

The correct anisotropy parameter is
\[
\boxed{
\gamma(\hat n)=
\frac{Z+\kappa C_\parallel(\hat n)}
{Z+\kappa C_\perp(\hat n)}.
}
\]

Equivalently, with
\[
\boxed{
x=\frac{\kappa C_\perp(\hat n)}{Z},
}
\]
one has
\[
\boxed{
\gamma=\frac{1+r x}{1+x},
\qquad
x=\frac{\gamma-1}{r-\gamma}.
}
\]

So \(\gamma\approx1.8\) is most natural when the defect normal is biased toward a \([111]\)-type direction, but it is not universal.
The previous use of \(r=4\) should be understood as the body-diagonal maximum, not the generic BCC value.
